I did as the person who stood before me had done, first handing over a 10-won bill. The other person, after confirming the money, hands over a newspaper.

Among the newspaper peddlers there, I, who was a little boy, grabbed the newspaper that was handed to me and went out into the street, and while watching what the other kids were doing, I imitated them and ran.

Tomorrow morning's Kukje Shinmun! A voice that grabbed my running feet was heard from behind, and a single newspaper was exchanged for 20 won.

I ran back to the newspaper office. The long line had all disappeared. Immediately grabbing two newspapers, I ran down the street, shouting unconsciously.

"Tomorrow morning's Kukje Shinmun."

I shouted until my throat was raw.

The newspaper sold well that day. I went in and out of the newspaper office several times. In my pocket, I came to have several 100-won and 10-won bills. But as time went on, the newspaper gradually did not sell.

I went around the outskirts of the darkening streets and sold newspapers. Even after the dim electric lights came on, I ran around the streets for a long time. I had no interest in how much time had passed. For the first time in my life, I was thrilled and happy that I was making money.

The money I collected that day was 360 won. When I went home, drenched in sweat, my sister-in-law grumbled, saying that a fool like me couldn't even come home on time. Even while hearing such words, my face didn't turn red like it usually did.

After eating a bowl of cold cornmeal porridge with soy sauce in a small earthenware bowl, I sat upright, and my sister-in-law, even more annoyed, had a venomous look on her face and was about to say something.

I didn't wait any longer and quickly put 200 won in front of such a sister-in-law. The moment she saw the money, my sister-in-law's expression changed in an instant, and a smile spread across her face.

"Where did you get the money?"

My sister-in-law, curious about the source of the money, asked me.

"Didn't I just sell newspapers?"

After hearing my story of the day, my sister-in-law seemed to be thinking about something, and then her eyes even teared up.

In my young mind, thinking that this woman was not a bad person, I was able to fall into a deep sleep that night.

The next morning, I woke up right away after hearing the all-clear siren.

With 160 won in my possession, I started running along the dawn streets of Yeongdo Island, watching the fading stars.

I went to the place where the kids I had met while selling newspapers yesterday said they went to get newspapers. Around the building with the sign "Dong-A Ilbo Busan Branch" next to the Gaedari Bridge in Toseong-dong, it was teeming with newspaper boys.

The children who had come first were blocking the front of the iron bars and had formed a long line behind them. I also stood in line for my turn. A line immediately formed behind me. Since they were all boys in the same situation, it was easy to talk.

"When will the newspaper come out?"

I asked the child behind me, and he said it would come soon.

Not long after, a bicycle came pulling a rickshaw full of newspapers. As strong boys ran up pushing the rickshaw, in the midst of the clamor, the children, like devils, stuck to the line, pushing to get the newspaper first.

When it was my turn, I received as many newspapers as the money I had. Then, holding the newspapers,

"Dong-A Ilbo! Dong-A Ilbo!"

I shouted and ran busily down this street and that. The child running in front of me shouted something different,

"Dong-A Ilbo! Special edition!"

At that time, it was an election period, so newspapers sold better than usual, but the news that Mr. Shin Ik-hee, who was the presidential candidate of the opposition party, had suddenly died, was a day for the newspaper boys to run as vigorously as the shock to society.

By noon, many people in coffee shops and stores were looking for the Dong-A Ilbo, and because there were not enough newspapers, the price of one copy of the Dong-A Ilbo jumped to 50 won.

Anyway, for the time being, the newspaper boys were excited. On that one day, I had managed to save 800 won, even after eating breakfast and lunch.

When I returned home late at night, I saw that the porridge bowl was different from yesterday. Unlike other times, it was not a rice bowl, but a larger earthenware bowl filled to the brim with cold porridge.

The next day, and the day after that, I went out as a newspaper peddler and tried to learn how to live in the world.

When the election was over, the newspaper did not sell well even if I ran hard. Still, I went out every day without a single day's rest.

As the weather grew hotter, the newspaper business became even more difficult.

Some places had a local turf, so I couldn't even go in and out. Not knowing that, I got into big trouble one day.

Without knowing anything, I sold a newspaper in a coffee shop and came out, but someone grabbed my wrist. I looked to the side and was dragged away by older kids and beaten up.

My clothes were torn, my whole face was bruised, and the newspaper was also torn. I couldn't even cry at that time because of fear. They even put a knife to my neck and threatened to stab me, saying they would kill me if I came in one more time. No one came to my rescue.

I was half-dead and couldn't even get up, groaning, but they spat on my body as if I were dirty and left.

After being beaten once, I became cautious and avoided places with a strong sense of turf.

The newspaper didn't sell well. And yet, my situation was different now. My sister-in-law waited for me to bring money.

I felt more comfortable wandering the unfamiliar alleys with a newspaper in my hand than playing with the neighborhood kids. In my mind, I only thought about what kind of work I could do to earn some money as a child.

One day, the newspaper was not selling well, so I was just walking along and spoke to a boy selling ice cakes on the street. He too, as his ice cakes were not selling well, was sitting on his box and just kept shouting "ice cake."

With the newspaper in my hand, I approached the boy. And then I asked what I was curious about.

"How much do you make in a day?"

He looked at my questioning face foolishly and said.

"Well, about 600 won."

He said there were people who earned more.

I became envious at the income of 600 won.

"Can anyone do it?"

I also asked how one could become an ice cake seller.

The boy taught me things like how much the box fee was and how much to pay when receiving the ice cakes. For every one I sold, I would make a profit of 4 won. So I made a promise with the boy. We decided to meet in front of the ice cake shop the next morning.

The 1,800 won for the box fee was a slight problem, but I thought I could manage by adding my newspaper business capital and getting some from my sister-in-law. And so, the next day, I quit the newspaper business and switched to selling ice cakes.

The people at the ice cake factory seemed to think I looked too young, but when I insisted I would do it, the owner of the ice cake shop also gave his consent, saying to do well.

The midsummer weather made sweat pour down my body, which had an ice box slung over its shoulder. It wasn't as easy to move around as it was during the newspaper business.

The ice cake box was heavy, and when I first started, there were many things that were more awkward than I thought. The most urgent thing was that the word "ice cake" would linger in my mouth and then disappear.

I carried the box, which was heavy for my body, and walked aimlessly.

Then, when I came near the city hall, I closed my eyes tightly, summoned my courage, and shouted "ice cake." At that moment, someone behind me asked for an ice cake.

I took out an ice cake from the box with a cool pose, gave it to the customer, and closed the lid of the box. At that time, the boy who bought the cake asked me.

"Do you sell 'keki'?"

I raised my head and faced the customer.

In front of my eyes stood a boy who used to live in the same neighborhood in my hometown.

My heart pounded as if I were a sinner. Heat rose to my face, and I couldn't speak. I just laughed awkwardly. He held out a 10-won bill. Only then did the words come out.

"Keep it."

But he insisted on making me take the money, left words of "sell a lot," and went elsewhere. Until he was out of sight, I stared blankly at the street, then felt ashamed of my own shabby appearance.

Nothing came to mind in my head, with the box slung around me.

I walked as my feet took me. The place I settled was the pier by the sea behind the city hall. In a deserted place with few people coming and going, I sat on the box and stared at the seawater for a long time.

No matter how hard I tried, my father's face, which I couldn't recall, and the image of my mother, who was never at peace even for a moment until she passed away, came to mind.

Thinking of the dear old days, tears kept flowing down my cheeks.

Occasionally, passersby would ask if I had ice cakes and buy them.

I was foolishly dazed all day, and a sunset began to form in the sky. Perhaps the cool sea breeze had stopped the sweat, or perhaps the passersby no longer wanted to buy ice cakes.

I finally came to my senses a little and was curious about what had happened to the ice cakes. I opened the box and looked inside, and I couldn't see any ice cakes, only the wooden sticks and the melted ice cake water that had turned into red bean porridge.

I didn't know what to do at times like this, but it was a waste to throw away the things I had invested in. I took the tin can out of the box and started picking out the wooden sticks. I thought I would drink that water instead of a meal.

The wooden sticks I picked out from the box were 23. When I drank the water from 23 melted ice cakes, the lukewarm cake water was a truly tasteless food. I packed up the empty box, slung it over my shoulder, and walked along the street where the electric lights were beginning to turn on, heading for the cake factory.

The factory technician saw me, a young boy, and asked if I had sold everything, but I couldn't answer. I filled the empty box with ice and received 50 cakes in the box again.

Looking at the lights of the village on the outskirts in the distance, I raised my voice and shouted into the darkness.

"Ice cake, delicious ice cake."

The obsession to sell even one more made me run around breathlessly.

My body, which hadn't eaten anything all day, was on the verge of death. And yet, my mouth shouted out.

Even though I hadn't eaten anything, my stomach made a strange noise. It gurgled and rumbled, and sometimes it made the sound of a machine gun I heard during the Korean War.

As time passed, my stomach churned even more, and I even felt pain. And yet, all I could think about was worrying about losing my investment, so I moved and shouted like crazy.

I had to sell all the ice cakes quickly, but the melted cake water I drank in the evening seemed to be bad for my body. I endured the unbearable pain, even breaking out in a cold sweat, and got through the night.

After one night, the pain stopped, but my whole body was weak and a wreck. Thinking that it would be okay after a while, I didn't want to miss a single day of the business I had started with such difficulty, so I went out to the ice cake factory again.

In the scorching heat of a hot summer day, I diligently filled my time with the ice cake business.

But as summer passed and I felt a cool breeze, the ice peddlers on the street disappeared one by one. Most of the ice cake sellers hurried to change their business.

Now, what should I do, I thought to myself. I wanted to do anything. But no matter how hard I thought about what I should do, nothing came to mind right away. At a loss, I wandered the streets looking for a worthwhile job.

There weren't many jobs in the world for a 13-year-old boy. One day, I went up to the top of Gogal Mountain in Yeongdo, where there were no people, by myself. I wanted to dig up a single kudzu root, but I couldn't find what I was looking for anywhere.

Looking out at the distant sea, I took a deep breath. In the distance, other islands faintly came into view. In the closer sea, I could see boats coming and going, and around the island, I could see something moving.

Out of curiosity and thinking that something might happen if I went down there, I ran down the pathless mountain slope.

Whistling a long breath, the female divers were working in the water off the coast. They would float on the water, then dive down, and after staying in the water for about 1-2 minutes, they would come out.

Since I had nothing else to do, I kept watching them work, and when the women came out, they all carried net bags full of seafood, which they carried with difficulty.

The things they had caught were things sold at the market and seemed like they would be worth a considerable amount of money. It seemed that anyone could work in the sea, as if it had no owner. There were quite a lot of female divers.

After approaching the female divers, listening to their stories, and seeing the things they had worked on, I thought on my way home that I should find some kind of work in the vast, ownerless sea.

That evening, I got a 30-won pair of diving goggles from a stationery store in front of the village, and worried that the goggles might leak, I spent the whole night sealing the gap between the tin and the glass with candle wax.

The next day, as soon as it was light, after eating a bowl of porridge for breakfast, I got a flour sack, rolled it up, tucked it under my arm, and went to a deserted beach. To find a place with no people coming and going, I had to cross a rough mountain slope with no path and a dangerous cliff several times.

7. The Autumn Sea

The sea water after Chuseok was cold.

When I finally decided on a place and tried to start working, the blue water brought a sense of fear to my heart. But I took off my clothes one by one.

My whole body was immediately naked. The cool air made my body tremble. I tied the flour sack to a rope and tied the rope around my waist.

I jumped into the shallow water and thrashed my body. my whole body is submerged in water. I swam, moving my hands and feet. I adjusted the goggles to fit my face and stuck my head into the water.

I could see the shallow bottom of the sea. It felt like my body would get tired right away. So, I couldn't work in the deep sea like the female divers.

With all my strength, I swam about 20 meters from the water's edge to a rock that was visible in the middle of the water and seemed about to be submerged by the waves.

My breath came quickly. My hand grabbed a corner of the rock. At that moment, a wave came and tried to pull my body off the rock. I thrashed with all my might and took a rest until the next wave came. After controlling my breath, I put my head in the water and looked at the bottom of the sea.

At a depth of 2 to 3 meters, I could see the things I was looking for. There were stone seaweed and kelp-like seaweed. I put my face back on the surface of the water and controlled my breath. Then I plunged my head into the water and dove down.

I tore off a handful of seaweed with my small hands and came up to the surface. There were also top shells attached to the rocks at the bottom of the sea, and mussels attached to the rocks, so I could pick them. I made some things and brought them out of the water.

After grilling the top shells and mussels over a fire and eating them with raw seaweed to satisfy my hunger, I continued to work, and in a few hours, the flour sack I had prepared from home was full.

To return home, I wiped my wet body and put on my clothes, and a chill came over my whole body and my teeth chattered.

The fire I made from the wood produced more smoke than flames. Crying, I blew on the flames with my breath to make them bigger, and relying on those flames, I warmed my body and forgot all my worries.

Carrying the flour sack, which was quite heavy and dripping with water, on my shoulder, I returned home the way I had come.

Seeing the things I had brought, my sister-in-law looked at me with surprised eyes and marveled. My sister-in-law seemed to leave enough for us to eat and sell the rest to the neighbors.

Our family was also able to fill our stomachs for the first time in a long time with a refreshing soup with plenty of mussels.

The next day, I also went out to the sea and worked. My sister-in-law took the things I had brought to the market, exchanged them for money, and bought barley and cornmeal.

I felt proud even in my young heart that I could be treated like a person in front of my sister-in-law rather than just being tired, and it was a good feeling.

Some days, other kids from the village would come out to the beach following me. I felt how reassuring it was to have a friend next to me when I was diving in a remote place. On such days, I worked even more diligently, as if in a competition.

As November came, the temperature of the seawater became too cold to work in the water. The sight of the female divers working was also not easily seen.

I felt the limits of my courage and patience.

The village kids also didn't want to follow me to the sea. I had to find another job again and became a person who wandered the streets all day.

Life, even in my eyes, seemed to be getting tighter. My brother, as if he had no responsibility for supporting his family, couldn't even manage to feed us corn porridge.

But I was still too young to have ever resented my brother or blamed him. I had never once felt sad about my brother's behavior. Because of this situation, being idle was more of a burden than working.

The next day, too, I wandered around listlessly, looking around in all directions for a job I could do, from morning until late in the afternoon. I only thought that I had to find some kind of work soon.

It was one such day. For some reason, I felt hungrier than usual. I loitered around the fruit and vegetable market next to the city hall.

At that moment, in front of my eyes, a 50-year-old man with a scruffy appearance was struggling to pull a rickshaw loaded with cabbage for kimchi. People passed by him without anyone helping.

A woman with a frustrated face was busily saying something next to him. I approached him and asked. Shall I push it for you? At my words, the porter, who was straining, looked at me.

Perhaps because my frail physique was more unappealing than my shabby clothes, the porter didn't even deign to answer and tried to pull the rickshaw again with all his might. And yet, the wheels of the rickshaw wouldn't budge.

Without getting the porter's permission, I pushed the back of the rickshaw with all my might. The rickshaw then began to budge and the wheels started to turn. Without any thought, I continued to push the rickshaw as if it were my own work.

For quite a long time, the rickshaw turned several alleys and went up a slope. It was a cold winter, but sweat poured down my whole body. The rickshaw porter stopped the rickshaw and began to untie the rope that bound the cabbage heads.

Only then did the woman, who seemed to be the owner of the cabbage, stare at my face as if it were a wonder. Without listening to anyone's words, I picked up handfuls of cabbage and began to pile them inside the house.

When the work was all done, the woman gives the rickshaw porter 500 won. The porter, holding the money, looks back and forth between the money and my face, as if he is thinking for a long time. He takes out a 100-won bill and puts it in my hand, saying, "Here, buy yourself something to eat."

I refused several times, saying no, and then took the money. When I held the money in my hand, the difficult work of a moment ago disappeared from my memory.

I said to the rickshaw porter, who was past middle age.

"I'll pull it."

The porter smiled without a word, and I cheerfully pulled the rickshaw and went ahead. The porter hurriedly followed behind me and told me to be careful.

The two of them ran busily towards the fruit and vegetable market. The rickshaw owner, the porter, seemed to be thinking about something and then spoke to me again.

"Hey, did you have lunch?"

I hesitated for a moment, wondering what to say, and then

"I don't eat lunch."

The porter asked why I didn't eat lunch and asked if I had parents.

"I live with my older brother."

He sighs towards the distant space with an expression of having felt something.

Then the owner of the rickshaw took my hand, led me away, and insisted on buying me a 30-won bowl of udon and making me eat it. I felt a warmth from the porter for the first time in a long time.

I, a 13-year-old boy, became the porter's assistant, going around the fruit and vegetable market with him, approaching people who came to buy cabbage for kimchi, and asking, "Shall I bring a rickshaw?"

And so, I really did become the porter's assistant. I often took charge of the luggage. Even though it was hard, the thought of making money gave me more courage to push the rickshaw around.

The porter praised me, saying I was smart. As I thought about the small amount of money he gave me, I asked him again in the evening.

"How about I come out tomorrow too?"

"Yes, come out."

At the porter's consent, I ran joyfully towards the road.

The porter watched my back for a long time.

And so, I went to the market every day with the porter. But as winter deepened, the weather grew colder.

The footsteps of the ladies coming to the fruit and vegetable market to buy things for kimchi became sparse, and the trucks loaded with things for kimchi did not come into the fruit and vegetable market. The fruit and vegetable market became that much more deserted.

I felt that the time had come to part with the porter, who seemed to have a deep sense of compassion. He, who knew my situation to some extent, worried about me first without worrying about himself. He asked what I would do now. Then he let out a complaint.

"If only I had a son like you..."

He spoke of his own children with regret.

I said, "I will pray for something worthwhile to happen to you, sir," and we exchanged our final goodbyes as I left the fruit and vegetable market where there was no more work.

Even as I felt that I had said something wonderful with the words I had thought of, due to my young age, I ran out onto the main road for no reason, with a somewhat shy heart, without a destination.

And then, the thought that I had to do something again intensely shook my heart. I felt a shiver as the strong sea breeze hit my ears.

My aimless steps were climbing up the 40 steps. I looked at the newspaper delivery boy recruitment ad posted on the wall of the street. I began to read the recruitment ad posted on the wall, letter by letter, as if my eyes would wear out.

At that moment, I was seized by the impulse to become a newspaper delivery boy. I summoned my courage and pushed open the door with the sign of the newspaper company on the stairs next to the recruitment ad. Inside the newspaper office, there were boys who looked older than me and adults.

I hesitated, wondering who to ask, and walked to the desk of a middle-aged gentleman wearing glasses. Do you recruit delivery boys, I asked first.

The gentleman looked me up and down and then pointed to the person sitting at another desk. I moved over to that side again.

"Do you recruit newspaper delivery boys?"

I repeated what I had said a moment ago.

I was asked a few questions by that gentleman and answered them.

That person told me to prepare a few required documents. I came out of the door with the sign of the Dong-A Ilbo Busan branch. And then I walked aimlessly down the street again.

8. The Newspaper Delivery Boy

In my mind, an image of me delivering newspapers immediately came to mind. And I wanted to become a delivery boy who delivers newspapers quickly.

My heart began to generate heat, even though it was winter. When my sister-in-law began to notice my behavior, I felt myself shrink like a sinner in my heart.

And then, after a long time, I told my sister-in-law about the newspaper delivery boy recruitment ad I had seen. At that time, my heart was feeling that I would never eat free meals.

My sister-in-law listened to my story with a calm expression. After a while, my sister-in-law, who got up from her seat, brought a bowl of pale cornmeal porridge into the room without any side dishes.

I struggled for several days to prepare the required documents and took them to the newspaper office.

Believing the people there who said they would notify me soon, I returned home and waited for news to come, standing in the sunny spot in the alley, enduring the winter cold day after day.

One day, after the students' winter vacation was over, the newspaper's notification for the delivery boy position that I had been waiting for so long arrived.

I ran to the newspaper office. I was introduced to the former delivery boy who would be training me at the newspaper office, and the next day, I began to check the subscribers' houses under the guidance of the former delivery boy.

With a bundle of newspapers tucked under my arm, I followed the former delivery boy, putting a newspaper in each house, and familiarized myself with the houses of the subscribers that would become my delivery route.

On the first day, it took 3 hours to finish the delivery. I had to go around every alley from Donggwang-dong 5-ga to above the Suwonji in Yeongju-dong. The handover, repeated every morning, ended in 3 days.

The former delivery boy, with a refreshed expression on his face,

"Do well!"

he said, encouraging me.

"...Yes, thank you."

I answered with these words and shook hands in farewell with the high school student who was the former delivery boy.

Now, I had really become a delivery boy, and in the early morning, looking at the sky full of stars, with a bundle of newspapers tucked under my arm, I ran around, looking for each subscriber's house.

As the days went by, the work of delivering became familiar, and the cold also softened, chased away by the spring air.

The newspaper was loaded onto a train in Seoul at night and arrived around 5 in the morning, so as soon as the curfew was lifted, I would get out of bed for delivery.

Since there was no clock in the house, I was always on edge for the sound of the curfew-lifting siren. If I missed the siren sound, I would often get complaints from the subscribers.

When I fell asleep early and woke up, the sky was full of stars. I, a young boy, couldn't tell the time, so I couldn't even get a light sleep in bed.

My only concern was to listen for the siren sound even in my sleep, so I was on edge.

Living like this, there were times when midnight was mistaken for dawn, and there were times when dawn felt like midnight.

I, who was deep in sleep, would unconsciously get up whenever I heard the siren sound. At that time, it was dark all around, and on the last day of the month, it was even more so.

It was one such day. I just heard the siren sound, got up from my seat, and ran down the familiar dark road, worried I would be late.

When I was leaving the alley, I saw a person walking briskly down the alley. Thinking I was late, I hurried my steps even more.

But the street became as quiet as death. Just as I was about to cross Yeongdo Bridge, someone called out to me from behind.

"Hey, come here."

Until then, I didn't know why the police officer from the police station was calling me.

"Where are you going now?"

the policeman said from a closer distance.

"I'm going to deliver newspapers."

I answered and turned my steps.

And yet, the policeman called again. In a rather calm voice,

"What time is it that you're going to deliver newspapers?"

I couldn't understand the policeman's question.

"I came out after hearing the siren."

At my words, "siren sound," the policeman seemed to have figured something out and told me it was 12:30.

Only then did I realize that I had mistaken midnight for dawn. I had nothing to say, so a laugh came out. The policeman on duty at the front gate of the police station also grinned.

I had to wait for the time, dozing on a wooden chair at the front gate post until dawn, and only when the siren announced the dawn did I run towards the newspaper office.

On that day, I was the first among the dozens of delivery boys, and after waiting for a long time, the door of the newspaper office opened, and I was able to warm myself by the stove inside the office.

In my daily life, newspaper delivery also became familiar. I was often praised by the senior staff at the newspaper office for being diligent.

It had been over a month since I started delivering newspapers. Now the day had come for me to receive my delivery fee. With a happy heart, I was thinking about where to spend the money once I received it.

My heart became flustered from that afternoon. My superior, the delivery director, tore off as much of the delivery fee as I was supposed to receive from the unpaid receipts, from among the receipts that were not collecting well.

My heart felt as if all my expectations until now had crumbled. Even though I was thinking that I had to have some money at home, as they were waiting for me to bring it, no other method came to mind.

With listless steps, I set out with the receipts, and in a hurry, I went to the houses of the subscribers whose subscription fees were overdue. The subscribers all said the same thing, to wait a few more days.

No matter how much I ran around, I couldn't collect from a single house that day, and I turned my powerless steps back home.

My sister-in-law, seeing me dejected, looked at me with suspicion, and my incompetent brother, for some reason, cursed and told me to quit everything.

I felt so embarrassed, as if I had made a big mistake, and wanted to get through that moment somehow.

The sufferings and trials of the world made me even more dispirited at such moments. Even though water was seeping through my torn rubber shoes, I had no plan to change even one pair of shoes.

Then my position in the house became even worse. The porridge bowl had changed. What had been in an earthenware bowl was changed to a bowl.

I felt hungrier and hungrier day by day. My body was trying to grow, but I couldn't fill my stomach, and enduring hunger became like a habit.

Even in the midst of the tedious things that repeated themselves, the hope that I had to live was what moved me.

It was late at night, but I had to wander around the Yeongju-dong area to collect some overdue payments. There was also a gukbap (rice soup) restaurant in the Chinatown alley that was two months behind on its subscription fee. The clock on the wall of the shop pointed to 9 o'clock in the evening.

The moment I entered the gukbap restaurant, the savory smell of boiled pork turned my stomach. Saliva kept gathering in my mouth.

Even while saying that I came to collect the newspaper fee, my gaze kept shifting to where the food was and to the people eating.

The restaurant owner, seeing me standing there blankly, told me to come next time with a displeased expression. I felt my whole body go limp and pleaded with him again to at least give me one month's worth.

The owner got angry at me and pushed me away. I stood there blankly, at a loss for words. The restaurant owner became even more aggressive towards me and cursed at me.

The people around, finding it hard to watch, told me to go back today and that it would be fine if I came and asked for it next time, and they all didn't even try to understand my situation and just scolded me.

No one in this world knew my pitiful situation. Tears flowed down my cheeks. Seeing me like this, the male owner of the restaurant became even more aggressive and even used vulgar language. I hesitated, wondering whether to argue back.

A customer seated me at their table and scolded me, asking how I could not listen to an adult's words like that. The restaurant owner also, when everyone was scolding me, seemed to have calmed down a little and became quiet.

I thought it would be better to talk about my situation, so I told them the story of how I became a newspaper delivery boy at a young age.

I told the people in front of me about what had happened today, not caring that the restaurant owner was interrupting me.

I revealed my pitiful situation, that this receipt was my salary and that I might starve all day tomorrow, and the atmosphere around me gradually quieted down.

The owner, who had looked at me so unfavorably, promised in front of everyone that he would definitely give it to me tomorrow, and the people around looked at me with very pitiful eyes. They really all looked surprised.

As I turned my steps and left the restaurant, I felt that the world's compassion was not as harsh as I had thought.

Having starved for so many days, I was at the point where I couldn't feel hungry even if I skipped a few meals. I only felt the instinct to unconsciously want and seek food in my powerless steps.

Every day, I still ran the same route, holding a bundle of newspapers as soon as they arrived. Some days, the train carrying the newspapers was delayed. On such days, the delivery boys had a hard time.

When they put the newspaper in, most of the people who were waiting for it would complain.

Some people would come chasing after me and shout that if I was going to put in an old newspaper, they would cancel their subscription right away, or they would threaten to not read the newspaper at all, no matter how much I explained, and they would hold me back when I was in a hurry.

I had to act apologetically in front of the complaining subscribers for all these things, as if it were all my fault. And the world's sentiment did not allow a boy like me to live easily. Difficult things happened more and more each day, and the hardship increased.

As the weather grew hotter, my body became covered in sweat. When I wandered aimlessly through this alley and that, I would always be greeted by the rising morning sun when I turned around the upper part of the Yeongju-dong reservoir.

It was an incident that happened one morning. The train arrived a little late. I was painstakingly putting a newspaper in each house. By the time I came up to the upper part of the Yeongju-dong reservoir, the morning sun was shining on the whole world, and the sun had risen quite high in the sky.

The people who were waiting for the newspaper called out to me and told me not to put the newspaper in. I explained that the train was delayed and ran to the next house.

But it was at that moment. My whole body went limp. The ground and the sky spun around. My head was so dizzy that I couldn't support my body anymore. Even as I thought that this shouldn't be happening, I couldn't find my balance. The next moment, I lost consciousness.

When I regained consciousness, many people were surrounding me. I was embarrassed by my appearance, even in my young heart. I gathered the scattered newspapers around me and ran with all my might, avoiding the worried eyes of the people.

After filling a year of my 14th year with newspaper delivery, my height had grown even in the midst of malnutrition, and with the experience I had gained, I felt a sense of余裕 (leeway/confidence) that I could do any kind of work now.

I handed over the subscribers' houses one by one to a boy in a pitiful situation who had come looking for a newspaper delivery job, just as I had volunteered for the newspaper office a year ago, in an area that was known for being a bad delivery route within the Dong-A Ilbo Busan branch.

The boy, who looked two or three years older than me, takes over with the same motivation as when I first started delivering. For 3 days, I went with the boy, taught him the delivery route in detail, and made markings that only he would know at complicated intersections.

On the last day, I asked the boy if he was confident, and he smiled and showed an expression full of expectation.

I felt a refreshing sense of liberation for the first time in a long time and parted with the boy.

It had been three years since I left my hometown and fought for my life at a young age in a strange city.

Around this time, the clothes my brother used to wear and pass down to me no longer required me to roll up the trouser legs or sleeves. The fit felt like a raincoat, but it was easier to move in than before. I often thought of growing up quickly and becoming an adult.

When I saw people coming and going, I envied the people in the crowd who had the body and strength to solve their own problems.

Even when I saw a man with a tan face carrying an A-frame carrier, or when I saw someone casually lift the curtain of a street-side udon stall and enter, I felt envious.

I came to have a small hope of when I too could go in and out of such places without any burden at mealtime.

My immediate wish was to be able to eat two bowls of makguksu (buckwheat noodles) in one meal. If I had another wish, it was to become an adult who could confidently do any job without being mindful of others' opinions.

I, who had no place to have any other expectations or hopes, no matter how deeply I thought, had no one to rely on but myself.

No matter how painful or arduous it was, I only believed in the fact that time would make me grow and get older.

If there was a heart that waited for something, it was only waiting for the passage of time.

The winter landscape by the sea was peaceful. Having quit my newspaper delivery job and with nothing to do, I once again sought out the quiet seashore.

Since it was a path I had been on before, I easily crossed the familiar mountain slope, and even on the steep cliff, I felt a sense of affection that I had never felt before.

A small wave comes to the edge of a rock and crashes, tossing up a white crest. And that crest immediately sinks back into the blue sea.

On the wet rock, stone laver was attached appetizingly. Perhaps because it had grown all winter, it would spread its wings whenever the waves lapped, and when the water receded, it would stick to the rock. I began to pick the stone laver attached to the rock as I pleased.

As time passed, the amount of laver I gathered also increased. Since the weather was still cold, I couldn't go into the water to work, so I couldn't collect various kinds of seafood, but I still rolled up my sleeves and picked whatever was within reach.

I put a handful of laver in my mouth and chew it. It doesn't have a particular taste, but for me who was feeling hungry, it was definitely helpful.

The things I obtained through several hours of work had piled up quite a bit. I wrapped them in the cloth I had brought from home when I came out in the morning, and it was quite heavy.

The next day, I also went out to the sea. The sea, with no one around, felt lonely, but I much preferred going out like this to receiving my sister-in-law's complaints or glares. The weather changed to be warm every day.

Even while going to the water's edge, my heart wished for the weather to get a little warmer soon so that I could go into the water as much as I wanted and pick more of the things I needed. I went out to the sea without a single day's rest. My skin got tanned dark red, but I had no time to care about my appearance.

Truly, it was a fortunate thing that my body, as if it knew my situation, did not get sick, and time passed by.

Some of the villagers thought it was not pleasant for me, in my shabby state, to even hang out with their children of the same age, but even in a place like this, the Japanese-born wife of a Chinese man who lived next door was the person among the villagers who showed me the most goodwill.

That woman did not socialize with the villagers. Her young child also always played only with his parents, like a foreigner.

I, as a neighbor, played with that child whenever I had time and also carried him on my back. Perhaps because of that, the child's mother, the Japanese woman, one day struck up a conversation with me. She asked if I could work at a cauldron factory.

The woman said that if I went to the cauldron factory, I could eat as much as I wanted. At the words that I could escape starvation, I felt that I could do any kind of work. She whispered to me that she would talk to her husband, so I should be mentally prepared.

Then one day, that Japanese woman called me and told me that the story was settled and that I should go to the factory.

I packed a set of old clothes and went to find the cauldron factory called Ssanghwa Jumul in Daepyeong-dong, where the Chinese people's foundry was located.



9. The Young Laborer

The large gate was open on both sides, and the ground inside the walled compound was completely piled with iron scraps. A passage wide enough for two people to pass by each other ran along both sides of the building, and at its entrance, an office was visible. Inside the office, people who all looked like Chinese were talking loudly among themselves in a language I couldn't understand.

I told those people the reason for my visit. A robust young Chinese man who could speak Korean, without much to say, told me to follow him.

I followed behind the young man. As I pushed open the wooden door from the outside and entered the building, it felt just like entering a cave.

The interior was pitch black, and there were no windows on any of the walls. In the cave-like space, two 30-watt light bulbs hung from the ceiling, emitting a faint light, and it was only after a long while that I began to see my surroundings.

The wooden floors laid on both sides seemed to be where people slept, and with enough space for a tall person's head to reach, another wooden floor was installed.

The young man told me to find a spot on the middle floor of the two-story structure. Then he had me change into work clothes there and come down. I did as the young Chinese man told me.

I climbed the wooden ladder, placed my belongings in what was to be my spot, changed my clothes, and came down. Experiencing this for the first time, what I saw before me was also marvelous.

I followed the young Chinese man again into the factory. A tremendous noise startled my ears and eyes. The sound of the blast furnace's fan and the sound of the engine that turned that fan completely swallowed the sound of people's chatter.

I saw people covered in dust all over their bodies. A hot heat immediately rushed towards my body. A Chinese man who lived next door to me in my village was making a mold for a casting out of dirt a short distance away.

I was instructed on the work I had to do there by the Chinese man who was in charge of the foreman's work.

The first job I was given was pushing a handcart. I transported coke for the blast furnace and carried scrap iron. I also had to transport stone fragments that went into the blast furnace. I pushed the handcart precariously through the narrow passage.

The eyes of the Chinese foreman followed the people working in the factory.

A discharged soldier who looked quite strong was also pushing a handcart, doing the same work as me. That person also seemed to be struggling, as if the work was too strenuous for his body.

I too, on the first day, managed to get through it safely due to my tension and my resolve to endure, but after a day had passed, I felt that it was certainly labor that was too much for my 15-year-old physical strength to handle.

And yet, what made me endure it by force was the lingering thought that although there were no side dishes, I could eat as much rice as I wanted. The bean sprout soup, which was not properly seasoned, was more delicious to me, who had lived a life of hunger, than the delicacies of the rich.

The hard work ends only when the sun sets. I had to look at the sun in the sky several times while getting through the day, wondering why time passed so slowly.

Only after it got dark did I wash off the dust on my body and was liberated from labor, but I felt a fatigue as if my body were crumbling.

I lay down, having forgotten my hunger for the first time in a long time, but I couldn't fall asleep. I had to struggle for a long time to fall asleep. A bustling atmosphere lets me know that morning has come.

People were hurrying to go out to work. I washed my face and, like yesterday, took as much rice as I could eat from the rice container with my own hands. I could eat until I was full, but now I had to feel a pain as if my whole body were being torn apart, which I had never felt before.

Among the 80 or so laborers, I was the youngest and looked to be of a weak constitution. But no one around me thought my situation was pitiful or sympathized with me.

The people working here were all poor people living arduous lives, people who couldn't even resolve their own exhausted bodies.

After a few days, my nose would bleed every morning. It was unbearably sad to think that the flowing blood was leaving my body.

But no matter how painful it was, and no matter how hard it was to bear, I had to do a full day's work of a grown man. If I was even a little lazy, the eyes of the Chinese foreman would follow me from behind.

Because the work was so hard, some people couldn't bear it anymore and left the factory, while others came looking for a job, even this kind of heavy labor.

During work, if even a little molten iron spilled on the ground, the person who was working would get hurt. Molten iron is so hot that if it touches the skin, the part it touches immediately burns. Anyone who worked here for a long time could not avoid having marks from being burned by molten iron.

On my body, too, some molten iron marks had appeared at some point. At those times, I had no choice but to endure it. As I did so, payday would come around. When I thought of the small amount of money, the faces of the people waiting for that money would come to my mind.

My sister-in-law's hope was in me, between her and her husband who had no ability to make a living. They were not trying to take care of me, but I had been taking care of them, and I knew it was my situation to take care of them a little.

My sister-in-law had given birth to a baby, so it seemed their circumstances were even tighter these days. Even when it wasn't payday, there were times when my sister-in-law would come to see me because the family's situation was urgent.

On the day I received my first salary, besides buying a set of work clothes made from dyed military uniforms, I handed over all the remaining money to my sister-in-law. When she received the money, her attitude was very different from when she used to consider me a burden.

In my heart, even after experiencing such things, there was no such thing as hatred. I only endured the difficult days, which were hard to bear, becoming a fool in my heart and quickly forgetting the difficult things.

After the day's work was done, when all the laborers in the factory dormitory went out and the train-tunnel-like dormitory was empty, there were many times when I was left alone in it.

I didn't even want to think about going out. I had nowhere to go, but also because there was nowhere to get comfort.

Really, most of the people working in this factory, in my young eyes, I couldn't tell if they were working for hope or for hunger. Enduring hardship was the means of the people here.

A certain 50-year-old Chinese man who slept across from my bed would obey the work assigned to him without straightening his back once all day, and he would spend all the small amount of money he received for it on opium injections.

Even while earning money so hard, that money was not enough to even buy opium powder, and seeing him struggle, I couldn't understand the ways of the world.

The Chinese laborer, who seemed to have been an opium addict for a long time, after the people in the dormitory went out, would sit alone under the dim electric light, stick a syringe into his own arm with one hand, and then, with a peaceful expression, close his eyes and lie down.

I, who happened to be watching this from right across, did not know what the old man was doing. When his continuous behavior was strange, I asked a senior laborer who was in the next seat, and I heard him simply say he was an opium addict.

It seemed that the senior workers here all knew about it before me.

And yet, none of the people here tried to stop this. And the Chinese people in the office were also the same as the other laborers. No one was interested in other people's affairs.

The people who worked in the factory, their simple-looking faces and actions were different, and they were good at saying things they didn't mean, or they would try to hide their own situations and just let them pass.

When I was together in this environment, my day also passed in hardship and sorrow.

The Chinese foreman one day moved me from pushing a handcart to being a stoker at the blast furnace, changing my job. The sound of the fan, which was constantly heard from the side, became louder, and what was immediately unbearable was the heat.

Just saying it was hot was not enough; it was just like my body was being boiled. Sweat immediately flows, and the body becomes as if it had fallen into water.

To scatter this heat in all directions, a fan blade larger than a person's body turns all day behind my back, so I couldn't breathe because of the dust from coal powder and iron powder.

I had to cover my mouth with a mask that even covered my nose, tie it again with a towel over it, and work with only my eyes exposed.

When work was in full swing, if I was even a little lazy, I would hear shouts that the molten iron was not coming out. Difficult tasks have no reason. Working harder is the solution.

Neither the Chinese nor the Korean laborers argued against the words of the foreman who ran the factory. The people who could not leave the factory even if their bodies were tired had their own reasons, and so obedience itself was the method.

After changing jobs, when I woke up, phlegm would come up from my throat in the morning, and the phlegm was mixed with coal powder and dust and was coughed up. The thought that this phenomenon would not be good if it continued for a long time came to my mind, but when the day broke, I had to go to do the same work again.

Such things continued for half a year. It was one evening. In the train-tunnel-like dormitory, as usual, many laborers were out. On the floor across from me, the opium-addicted Chinese man, who was suffering because his opium had run out, died.

People came back from their outing without knowing he was dead and slept next to him, and in the morning, they went out to work in the factory. When the Chinese man didn't get up from his bed, the news that he had died spread through the factory around lunchtime.

A handcart puller, receiving a few pennies from the factory office, wrapped the corpse, which had died from opium withdrawal, in a straw mat, loaded it onto a handcart, and left the factory gate.

Even though it was the day's event, none of the laborers talked about the dead man. It was as if it had been a long time ago, and it had been instantly erased from everyone's memory.

Inside the factory, the eyes of the Chinese foreman would flash everywhere when work began, and for a low-level laborer like me who had to work without a break, there was not even a moment to sigh.

Then one day, my sister-in-law and my older sister came to see me. During lunch break, someone said I had a visitor, so I went outside the factory. When my sister-in-law saw me, she turned her head, and my sister immediately shed tears.

My heart also clenched and tears were about to flow, but with the thought that I shouldn't be like this, I endured it by force and brought out the words. How did you come, I asked first. I wondered if they had come for money.

When passersby looked at us, I felt like I had become an animal in a zoo, so I urged them to just go up, and left the words that I would go up to my brother's house this evening, and I turned my steps towards the inside of the factory first.

In the afternoon, my idle thoughts are quickly erased by the busy work. In the evening, after work, I washed my whole body clean with a piece of laundry soap. For the first time in a long time, I thought about putting on the new clothes I had bought, which were dyed military uniforms, and going out.

That evening, instead of rice, black bread was served for the meal. I wrapped my share of two large loaves of bread in paper and left the factory to go to my brother's house.

My sister was there too. I presented the two loaves of bread I had brought to the people in the room. The people kept talking while tearing and eating the bread. They even said the bread was quite delicious. My nephew, who was over a year old, grabs a large loaf of bread.

My sister asks me if I'm going to let a spider spin a web in my living mouth and advises me to try another job. My sister-in-law, after hearing about the work in the factory at that time, for the first time spoke humanly, asking if it would be better to quit if it was that kind of work.

I expressed my intention to quit after working only until the Lunar New Year, which was about a month away.

I didn't come home until late at night when I was about to return to the dormitory. As winter approached, work became much easier than in summer, but as the New Year approached, the laborers in the factory began to get excited.

I wondered if the people who were all leaving for the holidays would return to this hellish place. One by one, as the New Year approached, people left the factory.

I also, with three days left before the Lunar New Year, told the foreman that I had to go to my hometown. I settled the account at the office and got my money.

After packing my clothes in a bundle and leaving the dormitory, the Chinese foreman smiled for the first time and told me to come back to work at the factory after the New Year's holiday in my hometown. I nodded my head.

Nothing came to mind in my head as I left the factory gate. My steps grew farther and farther from the factory. Looking at the chimney, from which black smoke was still coming out, I thought of myself as admirable for the first time. I thought that I had endured well during the past time.

Now, the day of becoming sixteen years old was only a few days away.

I felt hungry from the very next day. But I decided not to think about the nightmarish work at the cauldron factory again.

The January cold made my body shrink. There was no job available right away, and my body, which had to wander the streets all day, was relentlessly battered by the cold wind. I keenly felt the cold and hunger.

It was one such day. In the alley's hole-in-the-wall store, puffed rice snacks were popular with children. Shrewd people began to make puffed rice snacks on the street. I got a job in a place like this.

All I had to do was grab the handle of an old jeep and turn it and let it go. I put a little rice in the mold heated over a briquette fire, closed the lid of the mold, and put it in the place equipped under the handle, and then I would turn the handle to compress the mold.

Then, when I released the force, the small grains of rice would pop out of the compressed mold and become large snacks.

Turning the handle all day, my hands became stiff and my body was tired, but I was satisfied with the fact that I could work and that I could earn a small amount of money.

I worked diligently at this job and gained trust. Perhaps because of that, this house and that house looked for me, so I was able to work without a day's rest.

I felt the passing of the seasons just by the setting of the sun each day. I forgot sadness, cold, and hunger.

Just as the boys in the village called me 'matchstick', I was thinner than any boy on the street and only my height was respectable.

Because of my appearance, if I could achieve it right now, my wish was to be in a situation where I could eat two bowls of udon in one meal, and there was something else I wanted to say,

I only wished that my body, although not good-looking, would gain some weight so that I would not be looked down upon by others.

They were all ridiculous words if someone else knew, but for me, these two things were earnest wishes. Sometimes, I would blindly pray to God for my pitiful situation.

In the city, winter passed and spring came. Puffed rice snacks were once so popular, but they went out of fashion with people, and the houses that made puffed rice snacks closed down here and there.

I, who was in the midst of a growth spurt, had to find a new job because of the surrounding gazes and hunger. In my situation, there was no one to consult with, let alone someone who could tell me where to find a job.

The only thing I could do was wander the streets looking for a place that wanted me. I wandered around various places in the city, restlessly, with aimless steps all day.

Then, I reached the front of the US Information Service in Daecheong-dong, and while steadying my tired and hungry body under the strong midday sun, the black brushstrokes on a white paper attached to the wall of a certain house came into my view.

I moved my steps towards that place. As if by premonition, the paper had the content of a recruitment ad written on it. The recruitment ad for employees did not need me. But I could not give up after seeing a recruitment ad I had found after a long time.

My head was briefly dizzy. A desire to try and see arose suddenly.

I came home and spent the whole night writing a resume, wasting several sheets of paper. Although my face was not good-looking, I washed off the dirt with laundry soap.

I borrowed the pants of a friend of my age from next door and put on a white shirt that my brother had taken off to wash.

I pushed open the door with the sign of the Chosun Ilbo Busan branch in Daecheong-dong, holding a resume that I had fabricated to fit the conditions of the recruitment ad, not my own history.

The age was written as three years older, and on top of that, the resume, which was written with fabricated contents such as a high school education, which was not true, had many deficiencies compared to the requirements they wanted.

At that time, Mr. Gwak Do-san, the head of the Chosun Ilbo Busan branch, took the documents from the general affairs manager, looked at my face for a long time, and then unexpectedly asked if I could do it well.

Hearing my trembling answer, that person told me to come to work from tomorrow morning. The salary was 15,000 won at the time, and he said he would also give a bonus when I did well.

When I came out of the office of the Chosun Ilbo Busan branch, I was proud of myself. It was to the point where I wondered if it was a dream or reality.

That evening, I told my sister-in-law, who for some reason had a displeased expression, that I had gotten a job and that I would be receiving a salary of 15,000 won, and she too, with a bright face, asks if it's really true.

When I told her the story of how I got the job at the company, she worried that I would need clothes to go to work right away. That evening, I wore the clothes I had bought when I was working at the Chinese cauldron factory, which were dyed military uniforms, and went to work the next day.

The first job I was given was to supervise the newspaper delivery boys and check the subscription status of our readers. I was in charge of the general affairs for the western district and immediately became active.

I followed the delivery boys, checked the subscribers' houses in my district one by one, and had to put them in my head.

Here, too, I was the youngest among the employees who were doing the same work. In reality, half of the delivery boys were older than me.

To compensate for this shortcoming, I worked harder than others. At night, I would buy a book of a thousand characters and concentrate on learning Chinese characters.

While I was striving to make up for my own weaknesses, in less than two months, I heard from several people, especially the branch manager there, that I was diligent.

I took the delivery boys, who were older than me and in their third year of high school, around to urge them to pay their overdue bills, and I was not lazy in expanding the number of new newspaper subscribers.

So, I ranked first in the collection and expansion of subscriptions by district within the branch. The branch manager began to trust me anew. I became more accustomed to the work of the newspaper company. My fellow employees, who were much older than me, treated me kindly.

I felt a sense of accomplishment every day. But my brother, who was a heavy gambler and did not know how to take care of his family, often came to the office and asked me to lend him money.

Even though I sent him money when I received my salary, my brother's requests became more frequent. Some days, he would even show tears in front of me, a young boy, when I didn't lend him the money the delivery boys had collected.

If the conditions of his request were not met, it was common for him to use harsh words and curse. I felt troubled whenever I saw my brother. Poor brother, because of this brother, I couldn't even buy a single set of clothes even after several months had passed. I was always busy.

As April came, students filled the streets day after day, shouting slogans, and the high school students in the city competed as if in a race, filling the streets and demonstrating. The newspaper exaggerated these facts, and the excited citizens applauded the demonstrators, and day after day, there were outcries.

So the April 19 Revolution happened. Martial law was declared in the city, and armed soldiers were seen on every street corner. People became even more extreme. This behavior may have been people's stubbornness.

In the days that excited people's hearts, statements were posted on the streets, but people didn't even look at them.

People who have not experienced the future, without deciding what will become of them, the number of demonstrators who shouted "I will shout too because others are doing it" increased, and the excitement added to the heat in the spring.

On April 26, President Syngman Rhee issued a statement of resignation, saying that he would step down from the presidency if the people wanted it. A photo of him leaving Gyeongmudae was spread throughout society through the newspapers, and overnight, the ruling Liberal Party collapsed.

The world's sentiment changes again. The Kyunghyang Shinmun, which had been shut down for publishing articles opposing the policies of the Liberal Party government, was revived the day after such an event.

As the Kyunghyang Shinmun, published by the Catholic Foundation, was newly revived, a new organization and distribution network was formed throughout the country, and Mr. Gwak Do-san, who was the head of the Chosun Ilbo branch, took over the Kyunghyang Shinmun Busan branch again.

The Chosun Ilbo was passed on to another branch manager. Half of the sales staff went to the Kyunghyang Shinmun and half to the Chosun Ilbo. I went to the Kyunghyang Shinmun according to the personnel plan that Mr. Gwak Do-san had drawn up.

The sign of the Chosun Ilbo, which was in Daecheong-dong, was moved to another place, and the sign of the Kyunghyang Shinmun was put up in the same place. The new district I got here was the eastern district centered on Choryang.

Here, too, with my experience so far, I secured more readers than other places and managed it well. I handled matters smoothly with the delivery boys who were older than me, and my activities for the district also looked powerful.

It was a few months after that. The area I was managing was separated as a local branch. The local branch took over the handover. Now, I was working as an in-house employee at the branch and was in charge of taking over and managing insolvent local branches.

Since I didn't know how to be cunning, I only relied on the instructions given, so I didn't cause any problems.

Those who had been negligent in their experience with newspapers and in the management of the distribution process suffered losses in less than a few months and handed over their local branches to the head office. It was truly a busy year for me.

Now I was turning seventeen. One day, not long before the New Year, the Choryang branch manager came to see me. He asked me to look after some work for him.

And then he seemed to be pestering the branch manager as well. The branch manager, whether he was in a difficult position, asked me for my opinion and passed everything on to me.

The branch manager presented good conditions and pleaded with me to listen to his story, saying that the head office manager had agreed.

I, who lacked experience in the world, did not know what to do at times like this. I thought I had no choice but to accept the branch manager's request.

Following the branch manager's words that he would provide board and lodging, I moved to the branch manager's house. After a month, I realized that the promise was not being kept. I had been deceived by the branch manager's words and had put myself in a difficult position.

Even when I tried to leave that place, the branch manager's brother-in-law, a discharged soldier of about 27 years old who was known as quite a tough guy in the Choryang area at the time, tormented me.

I was suppressed by them for two months. In this environment, I began to use tricks to get out of that place.

From then on, the work I did was not so great, so the branch manager quietly let me go, but until the end, he threatened me.

Spring was slowly coming to Busan. I immediately became unemployed and had to find a job.

I went to my sister's house, who had moved to Yeongdo and was living there. My sister was happy to see me, but she looked worried, wondering if I had done something wrong.

I told her my story and expressed my intention to rest for a few days. And yet, I handed over the small amount of money I had to my sister.

From the next day, I wandered the streets without a job. I am wandering the streets to solve my own new problems. It was one such afternoon.

I happened to pass in front of the Gyeongnam Regional Military Manpower Administration office in Daecheong-dong. A new soldier recruitment poster attached to the wall caught my eye. I walked towards that wall. I came to feel that I should at least volunteer for the army, as I read the words on the wall one by one.

My feet immediately went into a public scribe's office in front of me and looked into the procedures and required documents for recruiting the three branches of the military.

10. The Boy Volunteer Soldier

I heard that the navy and air force were popular, so it would cost a considerable amount of money to apply.

I chose the army non-commissioned officer side, where volunteer enlistment was relatively easy.

After I asked the public scribe's office to write down the necessary information on the volunteer application form, the person at the public scribe's office brought me the receipt and informed me of the dates for the physical examination and written test.

When I came out of the public scribe's office, an image of myself in uniform came to mind. Could I really become a soldier?

I waited for the few remaining days, imagining myself as a soldier. After submitting the application for military service, my biggest worry was the physical examination. Since my chest circumference was less than half my height, I was too thin for a volunteer soldier.

Due to this situation, in the midst of the worries and expectations that arose, a few days passed.

On the day of the written test, I went to the Donggwang Elementary School playground, which was the meeting place located across from the military manpower administration headquarters, and about 100 volunteers were gathered here and there with gloomy expressions.

The receipt was changed to an examination slip. I thought that the other boys must have also applied for the army for some reason. When I actually came to a place like this, my heart was just gloomy. The written test was a common sense problem that was just a formality.

The next day, I had to undergo another physical examination. It was conducted at the 5th Army Hospital next to the city hall. As I passed in front of the examiner, I was very anxious that I might fail.

My frail appearance became a problem even when I came to a place like this. What bothered me was my chest circumference. I was burdened by the words I had heard from the surrounding volunteers that if it was not half of my height, I would fail.

When measuring my height, I tried to shrink down, and when measuring my chest circumference, I held my breath and tried to expand my chest circumference even a little. And yet, the record on the physical examination record sheet was precariously less than half my height. I was anxious.

All the procedures of the physical examination were over. The volunteers were told by the active-duty soldier who was their leader that they could disperse, and they scattered out of the hospital.

I felt an unbearable sense of loneliness. I felt that if I stayed still, I would become a cripple who couldn't even volunteer for the army in a world where there were many draft dodgers. When I searched my pocket, I had 150 won at the time. It was enough money to buy a pack of Arirang cigarettes.

I looked around and bought a pack of cigarettes. Then I ran hard towards the direction where the soldier in charge of volunteers, who belonged to the military manpower administration, was walking. Panting for breath, I called out to the first sergeant and stopped him. Fortunately, there were no passersby.

I spoke to him with a pitiful expression. I recited my volunteer number. Then I put a pack of cigarettes into his pocket and said,

"Please."

I smiled awkwardly and asked for a favor for the first time for something I couldn't help. The soldier walked towards the military manpower administration, and I turned around and walked in the opposite direction from the soldier, letting out a long sigh.

Now, facing a new adventure in my life, I spent days with a pounding heart. While placing great hope on a single pack of Arirang cigarettes, the anxiety of "what if" did not leave my mind. All my nerves were focused on the day the volunteer acceptance list was announced.

On the day the acceptance list was announced, I eagerly searched for my volunteer number on the paper posted on the wall of the headquarters. My examination number was among the numbers of other people.

Only then did my heart feel at ease for the first time in a long time, but a new worry and expectation made me feel strange.

The person in charge of recruiting army volunteers saw the boys gathered there and told them to gather at Busan Station plaza at a certain time on a certain day and then dismissed them.

I had no one to tell my immediate situation to. There was no one to dissuade me from joining the army after hearing my story, and of course, there was no one to worry about my future.

So, I kept it a secret in my heart, couldn't tell anyone, and just waited for the time.

On the day I enlisted in the army, I went to my sister's house and told her that I was enlisting, but she didn't even say goodbye or tell me to take care of myself. I went to my brother to say goodbye, but my brother didn't even bother to reply.

Even though I had been empty since yesterday, I skipped lunch and walked to Busan Station at the afternoon assembly time. Around the other volunteers, there were some people seeing them off.

I thought it was more comfortable to be around me, where no one offered words of comfort.

My heart tensed at the sound of the military recruiter's whistle. At the sound of my name being called, I answered loudly and stood in line, and for the first time in my life, I boarded a train for which the Ministry of National Defense had paid the fare.

As the whistle of the departing military local train blew, the volunteers on the train all looked outside and waved their hands. I blankly looked at the sky outside the window from my designated seat and just wished for today to pass quickly.

The train stopped at every small station and then left, so the local train made my tired and hungry body feel bored.

From the passenger car where the volunteers were, the sound of singing began to flow from that time.

The train that departed at 4 p.m. arrived at Daejeon Station only after midnight. The passenger car we were riding in was moved to another train, and then the speech of the people inside and outside the train was now different.

The passengers on the Honam line, with their Jeolla and Chungcheong dialects, made a person like me feel like I had come to a foreign land.

The speed of the train felt slower than when the locomotive going up to Seoul was pulling it.

Around the seats, some people were dozing, and there was also a volunteer who was snoring due to the effects of alcohol.

I heard a whistle in my light sleep. When I came to my senses, the surroundings were still dark, a pre-dawn morning.

The Honam line local train, which had left the two passenger cars that had carried the volunteers from Busan at Yeonmu Station, seemed to be relieved as it blew a long whistle and was seen leaving right away.

We looked at the stopped passenger car and felt that we had come to a destination where there was nowhere else to go. Soldiers in military uniforms began to control our group of volunteers from that time, and after lining us up and calling our names, they loaded us onto a military truck like a piece of luggage and drove towards the military base.

The soldiers in uniform used intimidating language. Our group lost our idle thoughts. We were to eat at the military base, where there was no need to pay money, at the right time.

The day after entering the physical examination center according to the schedule, our group's physical examination was conducted. Among the draftees, there were people who wanted to fail, so the sound of 'saba-saba' (bribery) was heard everywhere.

Among the volunteers, I, who was the youngest, was anxious that I might fail, even in this situation, and passed through each section of the physical examination center, holding my breath. I was very nervous. My hands were drenched in sweat.

My anxious heart, which had been on edge for two days, was finally at ease after receiving the 'pass' judgment.

The next day, like everyone else, I received an identification tag with a military serial number as the first procedure for a soldier, my registration as a regular soldier was confirmed, and the day after that, I left the physical examination center where I had stayed for a week and moved to the training center.

At the training center, as soon as the formation was completed, they had us take off all the civilian clothes we had worn and issued us with military supplies from socks to hats. We were also issued with personal equipment.

After changing into military uniforms, we had an entrance ceremony as trainees, and the next day, the training, which was the first process that new soldiers go through, was conducted according to the schedule of the training center.

The threats of the regular soldiers with rank insignia had enough authority to control a large number of trainees with just a single word.

Perhaps because of the tension that had settled in my body, every day was packed with training, memorizing military regulations, and cleaning and managing the issued equipment, but in my heart, as only the same things were repeated, time flowed in a feeling of boredom and hardship.

It was one such day. For some reason, after the training was over that day, the atmosphere around seemed strange. An order came for the trainees not to go out of the barracks, and the regular soldiers were armed and loitering in their respective barracks.

Did a war break out? Unknowable questions began to arise in my head.

The next day, I learned a tremendous fact. It was the fact that the May 16th Revolution had occurred and that it had succeeded.

One year after last year's April 19th Revolution, the May 16th Military Revolution occurred again.

Even while being tired from the scheduled daily routine, my heart was filled with curiosity about what was happening to the world in this increasingly unknowable story.

To put these thoughts aside for a moment, I decided that a revolution was not something I should worry about and forced myself to have new expectations.

Morning came again before us, and a day's training began. After waiting for the mournful sound of the bugle call for bedtime and spending the night, from the next day, in front of us who were trying to rest after finishing our training, one memorandum was added to our daily routine.

11. The Unmemorizable Memoranda

The revolutionary pledge, which was longer than the other military regulations I had memorized so far, had six articles, and during roll call, the platoon members would recite it in unison and memorize it. After a few days, during roll call, they would point out one person at a time and forcefully check their memorization status.

The other trainees memorized it well, and there were many who memorized it right away.

I wondered why the high-ranking military officers who had carried out the revolution were not trying to achieve their goals through philosophy and practice, but through written words. As I thought about that, no matter how hard I tried, I could not memorize this memorandum.

Every time I was pointed out, I couldn't memorize it, and because of me, one person, the platoon members received collective punishment. When this happened, the platoon members were all worried that I might be pointed out during roll call.

When I received the "Wonsan bombing" punishment for a long time, there were also trainees who resented me because of the pain. When things reached this point, I could not have good feelings even from the same trainees, the platoon members.

I was given the nickname "Gomun-gwan" (interrogator/nuisance). The frustrated platoon members would often reprimand me with such words.

At such times, some concern for the future of my country would not leave my chest.

I hoped that the military-born members of the Supreme Council for National Reconstruction would raise the country strongly and rightly, just as the six articles of the revolutionary pledge said, but I couldn't yet know their abilities and philosophy as politicians rather than as soldiers.

The revolutionary pledge, a song of praise for the successful, did not move me in my head.

Only with a silent devotion, while in the position of a private, I only hoped that the fact that I had been given the nickname "Gomun-gwan" would not be a shameful thing the next day.

Because of my behavior, I had caused the innocent platoon members to receive collective punishment several times. After that, the 6-week training was over.

The trainees' hearts, which had grown to hate the training center so much that the long, tedious early summer days would make them sick, all looked bright on the day of the discharge ceremony.

Dressed in new military boots and uniforms, we gathered on the parade ground and had a discharge ceremony to leave the training center. For the first time, our group received the rank insignia of a private first class with one chevron, and we were all so moved.

Also, after the discharge ceremony, military trucks came and took us to the discharge station. The people who were to leave as soon as the day broke stayed here for a short while.
