Some shrewd people were making connections to get assigned to a good unit, and rumors that if you spent a certain amount, you would be dropped off somewhere were openly circulating, but I couldn't use any influence for my own personal affairs.

Every time my comrades, who had trained with me and moved to the discharge station, received their orders for where they had to go and left, I also felt curious and hoped that I would be able to leave with the people I had become close to.

The remaining people asked each other about their military branches, and even in my anxious heart, I, who had no one to support me from behind, felt a curiosity about my destination, which would be decided by a special order from a higher unit, and only had another expectation of "what if."

It was one afternoon. The sound of a whistle was heard in the vicinity. The movements of the waiting soldiers in the barracks where we were staying became busy. A message was passed on to organize personal belongings and assemble. My name was also there.

We prepared to leave and gathered at the assembly point. We learned that an order had been issued for the group of the same military branch who had been waiting for 5 days to leave for a certain special school in Busan to complete their specialized education.

In my heart, after knowing my destination, even though it was Busan where no one would welcome me, perhaps because it was the name of a city I had lived in for a long time, I was able to feel an unknown sense of stability.

Our group, which left the discharge station, followed the leader's instructions, boarded the prepared passenger car at Yeonmu Station, and found our seats. The 논산 (Nonsan) that had been such a burden on our minds gradually grew distant behind us as the train began to run, spewing heat.

The military train was a local train that stopped at even small stations, so when it arrived at its destination, Busan-jin Station, it was already early the next morning, after a whole night had passed.

Our group, who had been sleeping in their chairs, all tensed up and got up at the sound of a shout, and the leader who had us get off the train gathered us in one place and counted our heads.

And then he pointed to the military trucks that were waiting nearby, had our group call out their names one by one and get on the trucks, and then checked the numbers. Only then did the trucks race through the city.

The busy schedule was also waiting for us at the special school, which was our destination. It was only after half a day that the platoons were organized and the barracks were assigned. Then, all day, we were tense and were given instructions on the formal procedures to be followed here.

The education here was different from the training center. Since it was only theoretical and technical education, after a few days, the initial excitement and tension disappeared for a while. Perhaps because of the summer season, it was difficult to fight off sleepiness during class time, and another pain arose.

Here at this special school, after a period of 6 weeks, the trainees were also allowed to apply for and be granted leave on weekends. But here too, I had a problem.

Since I had not yet memorized the revolutionary pledge, my application for leave was rejected for the first time. Every unit forced the soldiers to memorize the revolutionary pledge.

I, despite these unfavorable conditions, did not pay attention to the fact that I had to memorize the six lines of the revolutionary pledge. Another week passed. On that weekend, I applied for leave. The headquarters of the training company, perhaps finding my situation pitiful, granted my leave this time.

I polished my private first class rank insignia until it shone, attached it to my hat and uniform, and was cheerfully walking freely on the street for the first time in a long time when someone called me. I was stopped by a military policeman.

But I, knowing that there was nothing wrong with my attire or behavior, walked towards the direction they were calling from. At the military policemen's request, I presented my leave pass.

The military policeman on patrol, unable to point out anything, did not return my leave pass and dragged me to a certain place. The place where the military policeman took me was lined with soldiers who had had their leave passes taken away just like me.

The military policemen would unconditionally find soldiers who were privates walking on the street and control them. A military police truck immediately came and stopped on the side of the road.

On the truck, several military policemen, with fierce eyes, unconditionally loaded the soldiers, who did not know the reason, onto the truck like pieces of luggage and then drove off.

My cheerful mood of a moment ago became unpleasant. In the backyard of the military police station where the truck arrived and stopped, hundreds of privates who looked to be in the same situation as me, who had come out on leave from various units, were murmuring.

A single military policeman came running up and started shouting. He lined us up in ten rows. Several military policemen, with a lieutenant's rank insignia on his cap at their head, came and surrounded us.

Then, with the leave passes they had taken from us, they called out one person at a time. After confirming that everyone was gathered, they had us recite the revolutionary pledge in order, starting from the person at the front of the line.

Most of the soldiers recited the revolutionary pledge. The soldiers who recited it all were given their leave passes and sent out of the military police station.

In the end, because I couldn't recite the revolutionary pledge, the military police sent a notification to my unit, the special school, and my unit sent a car to pick me up, and in return, they canceled the leave pass they had given me.

I had my first leave since enlisting in the army canceled because of this.

After that, I was often pointed out during the evening roll call in the unit and received punishment for not being able to memorize the revolutionary pledge, and I was even made to receive collective punishment with the platoon members.

While such things were happening frequently, one day the captain of the training company saw me and told me not to go to the classroom but to stay behind. I, while wondering why I was being told to stay behind alone, went to the company headquarters.

The company captain and the regular soldiers stared intently at my face, which was frozen in a posture of attention, with curious eyes. A person called the training company's personnel clerk read something like an official document.

"For poor memorization, sentenced to two days of heavy labor."

He informed me that an order had been sent down from the disciplinary committee of the special school.

As instructed by the company captain, I took a shovel and followed the company headquarters' personnel clerk to the open ground next to the unit. The personnel clerk had me dig a hole in the ground.

The hot midsummer weather, in a short time, made my body, which was shoveling under the scorching sun, drenched in sweat. And yet, my heart, as I felt the occasional cool breeze, felt a refreshing sensation in my chest, which had been stuffy, every time I shoveled, for the first time in a long time.

Perhaps because it was difficult to stand in the scorching sun, the training company's personnel clerk who was supervising me just told me to keep digging without resting and then left the spot.

My leave was rejected every weekend. Lying alone in the empty barracks, thinking this and that, they will really carry out things like the revolutionary pledge.

They don't feel the duty or mission to keep their promises, but are they just making us, the privates, recite sentences of praise as a hobby? I wanted to ask.

In the last week of the 3-month training period, I was also granted leave. Although there was no one to welcome me, I walked freely, breathing in the air, wearing a military uniform, down the street where memories of suffering had accumulated in my heart. I feel like I want to meet anyone I know.

Now we had the last week left here.

Regarding the 1st Army and the 2nd Army, it seemed that all the trainees wanted to be assigned to the rear. From the stories I heard occasionally in the barracks, it was said that someone had connections somewhere, or that so-and-so would be assigned somewhere.

For me, who had no background and no one to provide money, I had absolutely no interest. The fact that I would be assigned to a front-line unit of the 1st Army was a clear fact, and this thought calmed my mind more than anyone else's.

I came to experience this thought of mine after the training at the special school was over.

At first, I was sent to Gangwon-do. After waiting for a few more days, this time it was Gyeonggi-do. What was strange was that the place I ended up at after wandering around like this was a direct technical battalion under the jurisdiction of the 1st Army.

But this strange life did not last long either. In less than half a year, I had to move to another place again.

After going through several replacement depots, the place I went to was a rifle battalion of a front-line division. I finally realized that I had come to a place that suited my status. Here, too, soldiers in military uniforms came and went as much as the unit's T.O. (Table of Organization).

While I was working with the soldiers who had been deployed first, I came to like the soldiers here. There were no soldiers who boasted of having a background, and no one who boasted of being the son of a rich family. Discipline was maintained by the dignity of rank.

At that time, in the northernmost part of our country's territory, north of the 38th parallel, among the soldiers serving, there were occasional deserters like in other units, but most of them, being in the same situation, still felt a sense of comradeship with those around them and were fulfilling their duties as soldiers.

By the time my rank became that of a senior private first class, I had become a rather strong soldier within my unit.

In the unit, soldiers of a rank about one level higher than me, such as a sergeant, could not challenge my cunning. Unlike other privates, I ate as much as I wanted, to the point where I didn't feel hungry.

It was after the company's cooks got a taste of my teasing, but I was in a position to speak loudly.

My superiors, the unit's first sergeant and personnel clerk, and the company commander, would often single me out for unpleasant tasks in the unit with a playful heart, but I later found out that it was the superiors' trick to tease me.

The more this happened, the more popular I became with my juniors, the privates. On the other hand, I was a headache for the high-ranking people in the company.

Even in the midst of this, as time passed, I was promoted to sergeant.

For the first time in a long time, I felt strength welling up in my body. Military life, for me, was the first happy place I had felt since I was born.

Then one day, I received a special order for leave.

The weather was quite chilly, and it was a late winter with a thin layer of ice still forming. The situation of the front-line units at the time, where the supply of goods was not good, was that they would store several sets of civilian clothes in the supply warehouse and only let the soldiers on leave wear them temporarily.

I received and wore those precious clothes, and although it was a small amount of leave pay provided by the unit, I even got some money and began to spend my leave using the military train. The only place that came to my mind right away was Busan.

So I used the military train bound for Busan. Even though I hadn't eaten anything since leaving the unit, I didn't feel hungry.

In the early morning, I got off the train and, while looking at the familiar streets of Busan, I thought of my siblings for the first time in a long time and started walking towards Yeongdo to find them.

Carrying a few packs of hardtack I had saved up and a carton of Hwarang cigarettes as if they were a gift, I went to my brother's house.

The 4-pyeong shanty, built on someone else's land without permission, was now swaying as if the rotten wood would fall off, and the expressions of the family members, who were wrapped in military blankets and trembling as the outside wind entered the room, looked pitiful.

Seeing me for the first time in nearly two years, their faces showed a happy expression, but it seemed they had some immediate worries. When I took out the hardtack from my bag, the children were very happy. The adults told the children to let their uncle eat some too.

I could immediately tell the situation here. I took out all the money I had saved and the money I had received for my leave. The family members, asking where the money came from, pretended to refuse, but it became very precious to them.

I felt sorry for my brother's family. Is this what affection is?

During the 20-day leave period, I became starved as in the old days, and when a job arose at a house I knew, I readily took on miscellaneous work, and the small amount of money I received for it, I handed over to my sister-in-law. Then, I faced the day I had to return to the unit again.

I bought a set of old military uniforms at the international market. On the day I had to go back to the unit, for the sake of my closest blood relatives, my siblings, I took off the new military uniform I had worn from the unit on my leave and gave it to them, and I put on the worn-out uniform and, without thinking about what would happen when I returned to the unit, I hurried on my way back.

The cold season, the difficult things, passed by, leaving behind a faint memory like an old story in a mind that was trying to endure.

I became a senior sergeant. What was strange was my strength, which had become stronger due to the military food.

At the division sports competition, I beat the other wrestling champions from each unit and took first place in the individual competition in front of many people. I felt that my body, which had been so weak when I volunteered for the army, had become healthy and strong in a few years thanks to the military food.

For the first time, I thought about what I would do for my country. At this time, my young heart was filled with only affection for my country and the feeling that I wanted to dedicate everything to my country.

In the unit, the soldiers' eyes came to regard me as a great person. I began to be recognized as a strong person in the vicinity.

Around this time, I also proudly took first place in the unit's regimental speech contest. My eloquence and strength were great among the soldiers.

My fighting skills, which I had protected myself with as a child, after confronting a 4th-dan taekwondo black belt who was the best martial artist in the regiment and who had looked down on me, and silencing his mouth, another myth about me became known in the unit.

No matter what kind of bluff I made, no one among the soldiers in the regiment said I was lying. The story about me became known to other units in the division as well.

The rumor that I was a man with guts, strength, eloquence, and a good head spread to nearby cities and civilian villages.

While spending my most energetic youth in military life, I could not be satisfied with military life.

Whenever a difficult problem arose in the unit, I was very popular among the soldiers.

Thanks to the people who knew me well and placed their hopes in me, I was able to handle any problem well. To the extent that it felt like the military discipline had become lax, the atmosphere was so good that the battalion I belonged to was even called a KATUSA within the regiment.

And among the hundreds of soldiers in our battalion, no major problems arose, and the number of deserters was decreasing.

I was treated as an 'exempt soldier' by the commanders and officers I knew.

My comrades began to regard me as that much of an outstanding person.

12. The Senior Staff Sergeant

I was promoted to Staff Sergeant.

The soldiers who had enlisted in the same year as me were discharged and left the unit. Every time, sending them off, who I had grown fond of, created a loneliness in my heart.

After the familiar faces left, new replacement soldiers would come, and while looking at the same number of unit members, I remained in the unit with my new comrades.

Time was raising me into a mature soldier.

And after a few years, I became a senior staff sergeant. The staff sergeants with the same rank insignia would call me 'hasa-nim' (respected staff sergeant) when they saw me, so I was a senior. And as the year passed, the staff sergeants were promoted to Sergeant First Class. I also waited, thinking I would become a Sergeant First Class.

A junior who was a few years behind me was promoted to Sergeant First Class. I wondered about the reason. Whenever a promotion opportunity came, it seemed that the candidates were using their connections for promotion.

When I learned this fact, I could not help but be disappointed with the military administration. A wry smile formed on my naive heart.

The new soldiers' eyes were amazed to see the Sergeants First Class fawning over the staff sergeants, but my heart was not so. Anger arose in me.

The performance evaluation was good, and the commander's evaluation score, 'very outstanding in leadership,' what happened to it? Every time I thought about these things, I couldn't bear the sadness that only I felt. I came to think a lot about myself.

So, the first thing I thought of was the proverb, "a monk leaves his temple," and I thought it was the best policy to be discharged, but no matter how much I thought about it, no good idea came to mind. My frustrated heart had nowhere to complain or vent, and my heart became even more troubled.

But I wracked my brain to find a way to conduct myself. Then I thought about the past. The old days of collapsing from hunger came to mind.

In the unit, where there was almost nothing impossible, I wondered if I could really solve the difficult problem in front of me on my own.

I immediately decided to challenge the destiny that had been imposed on me. I thought that I had forgotten my own future in the comfortable life of the military for the past few years.

Now I realized for myself that I had become an adult with labor power. Even if the army did not give me a chance to choose, I now had to create that chance.

Around that time, a special order for leave came down to me. I decided to take full advantage of the opportunity to take the last free ride on a military train to wherever it went during my leave. Thinking about things after my discharge, I went here and there.

On the early morning of the second day before my return to the unit from leave, I arrived at Yongsan Station by military train. Having no particular destination or business to attend to, I walked to Namsan Park to waste time.

The faint electric lights go out, and as the sunlight shines brightly, time is running towards noon. I went to Namdaemun Market and ate a cheap meal for breakfast at a cheap restaurant, and then walked towards Itaewon.

The place where I stopped walking was in front of the main gate of the Army Headquarters. A soldier who had only served on the front lines felt a sense of intimidation at the entrance of the building where the 4-star general was, at the guard post.

At the main gate, military policemen were glaring and on guard duty, while commissioned officers were entering and exiting the main gate like soldiers from front-line units.

I moved my hesitant steps. The military policeman at the main gate demands to see my identification. I entrusted my leave pass to the main gate military policeman and walked towards the main building.

In front of the main building of the Army Headquarters, I was at a loss as to where to go. Just then, a female soldier passes by. Flustered, I hurriedly followed the female soldier and called out to her. At my voice, the passing female soldier stops and looks back.

It was a pretty-faced female soldier with a staff sergeant's rank insignia. The other person confidently looks me up and down. So, a sense of bewilderment grows in my heart. My whole body and voice just trembled.

The female soldier, looking at me, who was looking for the Chief of Staff's office, with strange eyes, points to the second floor of the main building and tells me the location of the entrance and corridor.

I stared at the back of the walking female soldier like a person who had lost his mind, and then turned my gaze and went up to the second floor of the main building as the female soldier had told me a moment ago.

The corridor on the second floor was quite wide. I peeked here and there and then, in the middle of the corridor, I saw a sign that said "Chief of Staff's Office." At that moment, a sense of tension and bewilderment made my whole body shrink.

Expectation and hesitation held my actions for a long time. Thinking of myself who wanted to find something new, no matter what path it was, I started to knock. At the sound of the knock, the door opened from inside.

Will I really be able to meet the 4-star general today? The place I stepped into from the corridor was the secretary's office, and the chief of staff's office was through another door.

A lieutenant colonel in clean attire and two soldiers observed my movements. I saluted, saying "Pilseung" (Certain Victory), like a soldier. Then, calmly, looking at the lieutenant colonel, I brought out the words.

I told him the reason I had come to see the Chief of Staff. The lieutenant colonel, realizing that my business was about my personal affairs, picked up the phone and called the Army Headquarters' chief master sergeant on the exchange.

After a while, an old master sergeant came running. He instructed me to find out what my story was and to deal with it favorably if possible, right in front of me.

I felt that this moment was an important one in my life. The proverb I heard as a child, that if you keep your wits about you even if you are caught by a tiger, you will find a way to live, came to mind.

I began to speak clearly and articulately in front of the head of the Chief of Staff's office and the chief master sergeant so that they would understand.

I said that I was an orphan, an unsupported soldier, and that the military, for me, was the best workplace and a place where I could have hope.

As I said this, they didn't know the reason. I told them that I had a disease that I couldn't control myself. I also made up a story that I wanted to shoot a gun whenever someone shouted. And I said that the propaganda broadcasts from the South were distressing.

I put on a show, saying that I had to honorably retire from the military, having dedicated my energetic youth to it. Hearing my story, the two of them felt pity.

Finally, I plausibly explained the reason I had come, saying that I could no longer hide these things that I couldn't speak of, for the sake of myself and the country, and that I wanted to consult with the Chief of Staff, who is the highest person in charge of the military.

The chief of staff's adjutant and the chief master sergeant took a memo of my unit, rank, and name. They told me to submit a written request for discharge as soon as I returned to the unit. I hinted that it would be easy, as my status was that of a long-term service volunteer.

The Army Headquarters promised to contact my superior unit. In front of me, the people there called the corps and division on the army exchange.

I left the Army Headquarters building and walked towards the Han River with light steps. And in my heart, I believed that I could think about tomorrow's work tomorrow, and I was thinking that I should be happy today.

After returning to the unit and the day after my leave ended, I had the administrative office clerk bring a discharge application form. So, without anyone knowing, I wrote out the discharge application myself and requested the company administrative office to send the document.

The company's administrative clerk came to me and said that he couldn't submit such a document. Even when I said that it would be fine if he just sent it to the higher unit, the soldier who was the company's administrative clerk didn't believe me.

When I told him to call the division headquarters' personnel department to confirm, only then did the administrative clerk seem to call the division's personnel department's discharge section right away.

The people didn't say anything to my face, but in my absence, they sneered at my behavior, calling it an act of someone who didn't know the military.

Even in these days, as I thought that I would be discharged, as the days passed, while remembering the idle things I had done through my military life, a month passed.

As I was spending a curious and anxious time, I couldn't bear it and got a one-day leave from the unit and went to Seoul.

The head of the chief of staff's office and the chief master sergeant, whom I met again after a month, asked me what had happened. I said I had come because I was curious about that very matter.

The two men said that a staff sergeant's discharge order would come down from the 1st Army headquarters, and while saying they would give instructions, they called the 1st Army headquarters' discharge section. They didn't say when, but a message came that a special order had been issued.

Out of curiosity, I took a military train to Wonju and went to the 1st Army Headquarters.

The army headquarters was located in a wide area. When I went to find the person in charge, the clerk, although he was a soldier, treated a staff sergeant like me as if he were nothing.

I stated my unit and asked for the date of the special order. The soldier immediately remembered my name and his attitude towards me changed. He said that the special order would arrive at the unit in 2 or 3 days. He whispered to me to hurry and prepare to leave as soon as I returned.

I hurriedly took a car going to the area where my unit was located. That evening, I returned to the unit late and told my close friends that I had received a discharge order. Strangely, no one wanted to believe my words.

The next day, the rumor about me spread throughout the battalion, and the ridicule about my behavior did not leave the mouths of the soldiers.

It was only after three days of this that a message, not a special order, came down from the division headquarters. It was a discharge order.

The next day, there was an emergency in the unit. I said that since I was the one who had to leave, I would leave a little early, and when I said this in front of the commanders, no one refused my request.

The battalion commander shook my hand as he left for an emergency deployment. He said that if he had had time, he would have held a farewell party for me, and encouraged me to work hard and succeed when I go out into society.

I packed my simple personal belongings into a bag and came out of the empty battalion, from which the unit members had been deployed, and went to the regimental headquarters. The regimental adjutant, with whom I had been close, handed me the documents and special order I had to carry, ahead of the usual procedure, to leave the unit.

And he personally guided me to the regimental commander's office and had me make a discharge report to end my military service.

The regimental commander, while regretting my discharge, unhesitatingly said that I was a valuable person as a soldier. He encouraged me to become a great person when I go out into society.

With a farewell greeting to the regimental commander, I completed all the procedures at the unit where I had served as a soldier, and leaving behind the familiar faces and fond memories, I boarded a car bound for Seoul.

Now that I was going out into society, there was no one anywhere to welcome my discharge, and of course, there was no job that needed me. I, with a strong body, unlike when I enlisted, had only a calm heart.

My own effort and fighting spirit were all my capital and all my hope.

I did not purchase a ticket for the express train to save travel time, even while passing through Seoul.

Using the military train, which was my last chance for a free ride, I stayed up all night with the soldiers in the packed passenger car, with a tired body and mind. The train arrived at Busan-jin Station in the early morning.

Perhaps because it was late summer, it seemed like dawn would break soon. The area around the station was still quiet, and the lights of the occasional passing cars and the fading stars in the sky came into my view.

After thinking for a long time, I started walking without taking the bus that came.

The dawn of a summer day made me feel very refreshed. I began to feel hungry in my stomach, which hadn't eaten anything since yesterday afternoon.

The place I unconsciously walked to, as if drawn by a magnet, was in front of the house of my only sibling, my poor brother. His life still hadn't been freed from miserable circumstances.

I ate breakfast with his family and told them that I had been discharged.

I rested for a few days, then completely finished the discharge procedures at the reserve division, and then busily ran around looking for a job.

Since I had saved the money I had earned during my military service without spending it, I had a considerable amount of money on hand. I thought about what I would do with that money, but I couldn't find a suitable job.

I couldn't just play around. I was willing to do any work that would bring in an income, and I looked for a better job. As I had matured, I was thinking of leaving my brother's house for my own independent life.

It was one morning around this time. My brother and sister-in-law held onto me as I was hesitating. They said their current situation was difficult and asked me to live with them. And they kept urging me to let them put the money I had on hand on interest in a reliable place.

I, who was always weak when it came to affection, could not refuse my sibling's request. Without even thinking about the past, I entrusted the money I had on hand to them, listening only to their words.

We immediately found a house that was not uncomfortable to live in and moved.

For the first month or two, my brother's family was kind, and we seemed to get along well. As three months passed, my brother and sister-in-law's attitude towards me gradually changed. The money they had taken, saying they would put it on interest in a reliable place, there was no talk of the principal, let alone the interest.

Sometimes, for some reason, when my sister-in-law was in a bad mood, she would start a challenging conversation, saying that her siblings had ruined her life, and my brother would pick a fight, saying that the money I had entrusted to him was not even worth the food he had fed me in the past.

They now tried to drive me, who had become penniless believing only them, out of the house.

I had nowhere to complain about my immediate pitiful situation. My heart felt like it was breaking, and my vision went dark.


13. The Cold Society

My situation just kept getting worse.

A suspicion arose, "Could I have been deceived?", and for the first time, a fear of people in the world also arose.

I waited, vaguely hoping my brother and his wife would change their minds, but all expectations were nothing but futile thoughts. My situation became even more pitiful right from that moment. They picked a fight over every little thing.

As time flowed, it was no small matter of awkwardness for me to face the two of them. A sense of despair about myself arose first. No one in the world said a single word of comfort to me.

Just thinking about the past fates I had repeated brought about sorrow. My mind lost the will to even protect itself.

Then one morning, another incident occurred to me. As I was just waking up from bed and sitting up, my brother came over and started picking a fight for no reason.

He chose only words that were hard to bear and berated me. Still, when I remained silent, he said, "What a pathetic fool," and kicked me in the face. I was so taken aback by this unimaginable behavior that I was speechless.

I dodged to avoid the flying kicks and fists. My brother rampaged even more like a madman. Then he came in with a kitchen knife and swung it, saying he would kill me. I dodged the tip of the knife and went outside the room first.

He followed me, swinging the knife even more fiercely towards my body. My sister-in-law saw this and didn't even try to stop it. In a hurry, I ran out into the alley without even putting on my shoes.

My brother, as if he meant to kick me out of the house this time, followed me to the end, holding the knife and shouting that he would kill me. I barely managed to run a long way, shook off my brother, and when I looked at my shabby state, I was a complete mess.

The tip of my foot, which had been kicked by a stone while running barefoot, was swollen, and blood was flowing from between my torn toes. As the tension gradually eased, the pain was transmitted to the top of my head.

In a hurry, I went barefoot to my sister's house, who lived nearby. My sister, who had no power to sympathize with me, also lamented and only shed tears upon seeing my shabby state and hearing my story.

I had my nephew, who was in elementary school, go to my brother's house when he wasn't there and bring back my shoes and clothes. After eating a few spoonfuls of the breakfast my sister prepared for me at her house, I packed my simple belongings in a bag.

My fate was that I had to leave, even though I had no destination to go to. My sister, even while watching this behavior, just sighed and couldn't bring out any words.

Sorrow and despair began to pile up in my chest again. Tears flowed down both my cheeks, and the extreme thought of just dying this time even came to mind. I consciously moved my powerless steps.

At times like this, not a single place in the world came to mind where I should go. What I felt immediately was only the anger in my heart that had no way to be released. The simple thought of having to leave Busan was all that came to mind.

I walked towards the station where the train was. When I came in front of the station, as if it were a procession leaving for somewhere, a long line of people waiting to get on a vehicle was stretched out. I walk towards the ticket office and think. Which ticket should I get? I had to decide on a destination right away.

The names of distant cities quickly come to mind. After thinking hard, I purchased a ticket for the regular express to Daejeon. I followed the long line and went in. At the station, there were the gazes of those leaving and those sending them off.

The train blows its whistle at the scheduled time. I, who had taken a seat by the window, tried to forget everything by looking at the passing fields.

O cruel fate, O man born with that fate! Where are you going now? Don't spend today with futile worries. Tomorrow, the bright sun will rise again, I tell myself.

At that time, the person sitting in the next seat struck up a conversation with me. It was a country woman who looked to be in her 50s. The people sitting across from me all looked at me, who was silent, with curiosity.

"Young man, where do you live? Where are you going? You have the face of a rich man."

the 50-year-old woman kept asking, even though I didn't answer. The woman also handed me a boiled egg and urged me to eat it. I think to myself that there are good people like this in the world, and I just can't understand the behavior of my brother and his wife.

When the winter sun was setting, the train arrived at Daejeon Station. After leaving the warm passenger car with heating and standing in the cold, unfamiliar city's station plaza, the unfamiliar city looked even colder to me.

I looked around in all four directions, east, west, south, and north, looking for something. Which way should I turn my steps? A destination doesn't come to mind right away. In the distance, an intercity bus shouts for the passengers who got off the train, and a certain young man is shouting.

On one side of the station, there was a guide board introducing nearby historical sites. Songnisan, Gongju, Buyeo—I, who was hesitating, decided in my heart to rest in Gongju tonight and organize my thoughts, and then returned to the immediate reality. So I ended up taking the bus to Gongju.

On the dark night road, the bus shines its lights and runs vigorously on the bumpy gravel road. After making me feel bored for a long time, the bus stopped at a station in Gongju.

When I got off the bus, the shabbier-than-expected Gongju city streets came into view. First, I looked for a sign for a nearby inn.

The shabby inn room, perhaps thanks to the briquette fire, I could only feel the warm heat of the floor. This thought and that thought chase away the fatigue. I can't seem to fall asleep. After having all sorts of thoughts, the conclusion I came to was just to try visiting a nearby temple.

A person with no luck from their parents has no luck from their siblings either, so rather than enduring the quarrels of the world with a miserable fate, I wanted to become a monk and forget everything. I wanted to forget the memories of how I had lived so far.

I felt that the fate of a certain protagonist I had read in a storybook was like mine. When the day broke, the thoughts of which temple I should go to did not disappear from my head until it was almost midnight.

When I got up from my seat, the morning sun was brightly shining through the window, and the inn, perhaps because there were few guests, was quiet. I went to the well and washed my face with cold water.

And I hurriedly left the inn. What I could immediately feel was the hunger in my stomach, which had been empty since lunch yesterday.

My eyes diligently searched for a nearby restaurant. Perhaps because it was morning and there were no customers, the restaurants near the marketplace had no prepared food. In a house where not a single customer was visible, I asked for some rice in hot soup and reluctantly ordered a bowl of gukbap (rice soup) and ate it.

The restaurant owner looks at me blankly. I spoke to the owner first.

"Which temple around here is worth visiting?"

The owner, hearing my words, thinks for herself and says, "It's Gapsa," and teaches me the name of a temple I had never heard of before.

The conversation between the two people became longer and longer. The owner interestingly took my words and told me that the way to Gapsa was to take a bus, and even told me about the bus that was leaving soon.

After paying for the gukbap, I walked to the bus stop the owner had told me about to take the bus.

Just as I had heard, the bus to Gapsa was starting its engine and preparing to leave right away. I got on the bus and bought a ticket to Gapsa from a girl who seemed to be the conductor. Perhaps because I had come at the right time, the bus departed after a short while.

Thinking it would be right away, the bus runs for over an hour on the bumpy, winding road. After a while, the bus stopped on a forested mountain slope.

At the conductor's words that we had arrived, I got off the bus, and the people who had come on the bus said that it was quite a walk up to Gapsa.

I climbed the mountain by myself.

In front of my eyes, the temple's sign, "Gyeryongsan Gapsa," comes into view. In my calm heart, I feel a sense of wonder and loneliness about the fact that I have come this far. With every step, the majestic appearance of the temple comes into view.

My own shabby appearance, with a shaved head and wearing a monk's robe, appeared clearly before my eyes as if reflected in a mirror.

Now that the quarrels of the world are over for me, I moved my steps into the temple grounds.

First, while thinking about where to go, my steps were heading towards the main hall.

The image of the benevolent Buddha came into my sight. It felt as if the Buddha statue was saying something.

It seemed to be scolding me for not knowing my fate and coming only now.

Leaving my bag in front of the door, I walked in front of the Buddha statue. I placed 2,000 won on the altar and bowed. I'm sorry for coming late. Looking at the Buddha statue, I spoke in my heart.

Just as I was about to turn around and leave the main hall, a monk from that temple walks over. I think to myself that it's a good thing, but the monk, perhaps out of habit, keeps bowing his head.

I was about to ask him the way to become a monk, but because of his expression and behavior, instead of asking him to guide me to where the head monk was, I changed my words to something completely different.

"Where is the quiet hermitage here?"

I spoke as if I had come for a retreat.

The monk, bowing even more, points to the mountain peak where the clouds are hanging and teaches me that if I go up there, there is a hermitage called 'Deungwonam'. Kindly, the monk even guided me to the entrance of the mountain path leading to Deungwonam and then apologized for not being able to escort me further.

As I climbed the mountain alone, I felt as if the hermitage called Deungwonam would welcome me. With this thought, I climbed the steep slope, panting for breath.

Gyeryongsan Mountain, with its autumn leaves, also made me feel that it was a famous mountain.

I laugh at myself for leaving Gapsa and climbing the mountain. I wonder if I am not even fated to become a monk right away, and an even more senseless laugh is about to come out.

Thinking that everything is a connection from a past life, I climbed the steep peak for two hours. At the mountain peak on the side the monk had pointed out, I was able to find Deungwonam.

I went into the hermitage's main hall, bowed before the Buddha statue, and prayed in my heart. Please guide my future.

I decided to take a retreat here for a while. I wanted to think about my personal problems more calmly and decide on a definite path, whatever it might be.

When I looked for the head monk of the hermitage, a single monk greeted me. Perhaps because it was such a remote place, the people there greeted me with a good impression, even though it was our first meeting. We exchanged our first greetings.

I brought out the words I had in my heart, saying I wanted to take a retreat at this temple for a while, and asked if it would be possible.

The monk of the hermitage first talked about the problem of food and shelter, saying he would receive 25 doe of rice per month. I immediately said I would stay for two months and offered the price of a full bag of rice in money. With that, I became a member of that hermitage for a while.

The hermitage, which was said to have been built 500 years ago, faced Sindor-an, and the hermitage was steeped in legend. A temple worker, an old man named Kim who was a follower of the Donghak religion and had worked there for over ten years, kindly whispered to me.

He said that over the long years, the name of the temple was changed to Deungwonam 300 years ago, and that the person who wrote the hermitage's sign at that time had the same name as me. After hearing the history of the temple that has been passed down to this day, a strange feeling even arose.

The mountain top, where it was said that frost sometimes formed even in summer, was constantly swept by strong winds. I started a fire for myself in the empty room that had been cleared for me before it got dark, and while illuminating the room with a single candle in the pitch-black night, I was able to fall asleep, forgetting everything for the first time in a long time.

For a few days, the same life felt comfortable, but as time passed, I felt a longing for people.

On weekends, hikers would occasionally pass by along the mountain ridge, but when people who had come from afar to pray at the famous mountain occasionally stopped by the temple, I was very happy.

What changes in an instant is that desires and hopes are taken away by mountain life. I ate, slept, and felt a sense of nostalgia as I passed the day. When I got through a day with nothing changing, I smiled at the expectations I had thought of yesterday.

Besides fighting against a monotonous life, I developed a habit of wandering the mountains. If I crossed the steep mountain ridge, a shabby earthen house would appear one by one in every valley of Gyeryongsan Mountain.

I can see that things that are difficult for an ordinary city person to understand are still being done in places like this.

There are people who call themselves the head of a pseudo-religion without any followers, people who only pray to the mountain god to become a great person, and a self-proclaimed Taoist who has been living in an earthen house for 10 years to receive divine power.

On days when I felt like it, I greeted and socialized with these people, trying to endure the frozen winter of Gyeryongsan Mountain without being lonely.

On the days I met prayer guests from Daejeon and Seoul, I wanted to hear stories of the world, so I didn't want to leave the guests' side, whether they were women or men.

It had already been about 50 days since I started living in the temple. A few days before New Year's Eve, I felt a certain longing. Before making a final decision, an impulse arose to go down the mountain once.

An unknown affection stirred in my heart. For some reason, my mother's face came to mind. Such things kept making me think of going down the mountain. Then one day at dawn, I decided to go to Busan once.

On that day, for some reason, the cries of beasts were heard around the temple all night long. At dawn, on the path in front of the temple yard, two large owls were fighting fiercely.

Even when a person came right up close, the beasts blocked the path and did not move aside. Only when I grabbed the beasts' feathers did the owl fly into the air.

I followed the path down the mountain and arrived at Daejeon Station in the morning and boarded the train to Busan.

A feeling of regret for doing something pointless also arose, and I had the thought of getting off at the next station, but I held onto my heart with the resolve to really become a monk after confirming the faces of my foolish and unworthy relatives just once.

The train arrives at its final destination, and I hesitantly go to my sister's house.

After nearly two months, seeing each other's faces again, my sister, knowing my situation when I left all too well, seemed to think I might have done something wrong.

But seeing me looking fine, her expression was more worried than happy. When I told her about what happened on the mountain and said that if I went into the mountain this time, I would leave home, my sister starts to cry.

Perhaps because I saw my sister's tears, my heart, which was trying to be calm, also tingled, and tears welled up in my eyes.

My sister wiped her tears first, and whether it was true or not, she said that after I left, my brother also felt guilty and urged me to change my mind just once. And then she urged me to at least hold the memorial service for my mother, which was in two days, and then leave or do whatever I wanted.

No matter how many painful and sorrowful days there had been, I did not want to resent my only sibling in this wide world.

Perhaps because my sister had told my brother my story, on the day of the memorial service, he just smiled foolishly when he saw me.

Even though I had resolved to endure it for just today, thinking about the past made the moments feel more and more awkward. After the siren announcing midnight sounded, the memorial service table was set.

Even while hearing my brother's words to at least eat some food and go, I got up from my seat even though it was curfew time. The next day, and the day after that, because of my sister's words, I could not leave for the mountain.

After spending New Year's Day at my sister's house, I made a new resolution. Becoming a monk is something I can do whenever I make up my mind, and for some reason, I wanted to stay in the city once more.

I hurried to Gyeryongsan Mountain to pack my things and returned to Busan again, to start a gamble, betting myself on my own efforts.

To reduce contact with my siblings, I got a one-mat room on a mountain slope in Daecheong-dong, not Yeongdo, and lived there.

When work came up, I didn't hesitate to do anything. It felt like a single 10-won coin was precious, so there were more times when I went hungry and endured than when I ate.

In this environment, I was gradually building a sense of stability in my heart. Another year passed, burying many problems in memories.

Then one day, I had a dark night. It was when I had fallen asleep in a small room with the electric light turned off.

A strange sound began to be heard in my ear. At first, I tried hard to identify what the sound was.

But the increasingly clear sound was, "Dedicate your courage and conscience to your compatriots."

I looked around to see who was saying this to me now.

There was no one around. I opened my eyes wider and looked around in all directions. At that time, I could only see the shadow of the dark room.

And yet, the sound continued to be heard in my ear, which had been awakened. I truly felt a sense of pity.

It was hard enough to support myself, and now I was being told to dedicate my conscience and courage. It was absurd.

At that time, unknowable things happened. A sense of excitement arose in my heart, and my chest grew hot. From that time on, I could not fall asleep.

All sorts of strange thoughts began to arise, and I started to think about my own values.

This continued for several days. I finally couldn't deny myself anymore and began to look for a place to dedicate my young affection.

For the first time, an interest in other people arose in my heart. I thought about their inner selves rather than the outer faces of the people who had seemed happy until yesterday. And the causes of an unhappy society came to my mind.

I was gradually forgetting myself and beginning to feel that the greatest happiness was in saving others from their misfortune and that this was my mission.

A cry of "Compatriots!" burst from my chest.

I kept this to myself and ran around the streets to get bread. But that secret grew so rapidly that it became uncomfortable to keep it in my chest.

I started to cover myself with a blanket at night and shout. I firmly resolved to dedicate my courage and conscience to my compatriots. And my heart always felt like I was being deceived by someone.

Even when news spread that someone had gone bankrupt or that someone had suddenly become rich, people were not surprised.

The people did not seem to consider politics important. And yet, how will the world change in the future? Anxious thoughts made my heart anxious, but I had not even dared to think about what I had to do.

At one moment, in a dark room, I knelt down, clasped my hands, and looking for God, I brought out words of supplication.

"O God, please save my people!"

My mind became more and more dizzy at that moment.

From that day on, whenever I had time, I devoted myself to reading. I began to read things like the autobiographies of great men who had overcome difficult things on their own.

Then one day, I made a firm decision. I would dedicate myself to the bread and freedom of my compatriots, and while organizing these thoughts, I felt that if I brought out any words, people would point their fingers at me and call me a madman.

My absurd situation, the pitiful future of this society, was just so frustrating in my heart. As I thought about what to do, what to do, and as no one around me recognized it, I was afraid to bring out the words.

What should I say about the people who know love but don't know how to protect it? The more I thought about giving up, the dizzier my head became.

In Daecheong-dong, the reward for finding a room and working hard appeared in a year. A small amount of money had accumulated in my possession.

At that time, my sister in Yeongdo, whether because her life was tight, or because she was worried about me living alone, suggests that we siblings live together in one house. It wasn't that I wanted to, but I moved my lodging from Daecheong-dong to a small room in my sister's house in Yeongdo again.

Even while sharing a room with my elementary school-aged nephews, I could feel that I had improved compared to the past.

Is it because I've matured? A fiery sense of mission continued to burn in my chest, making my blood boil.

I couldn't bear it. It felt like I had to leave on a path of action with some kind of ideology. First, I felt that if I went to Seoul once, I would be able to know something. With a small amount of money in my possession, I took the cheapest night train to Seoul.

The train is packed from the start. It was a journey that was very tiring, as much as the fare was cheap. Sitting luckily on a narrow seat for three, I dozed off, and as the night changed and dawn came, the countless lights visible outside the train window let me know that I had arrived in Seoul.

In the early morning, I ordered a bowl of haejangguk (hangover soup) sold cheaply near the station and ate it.

The crowded cars and the newly built buildings made me feel like I had come to a foreign land. Now, how should I act? It was not a pre-planned trip, nor was it a trip to Seoul where I had made an appointment with someone. My actions had many strange aspects.

I walked towards the bus stop. The reason I searched my memory and took the bus to Moraenae was purely because I remembered the face of a man I had met a few years ago.

It was because I wanted to meet a young man named Gu Jwa-seok, who at the time had the title of interim spokesman, whom I had come to know through the introduction of Mr. Oh Jae-young of the Chupunghoe.

Some time ago, I had forgotten the address he had taught me, and after wandering around the Bukgajwa-dong area for about two hours, I finally found his residence, but he was out, and a woman who seemed to be his wife told me at the pharmacy when he would be back.

I couldn't just wait there with nothing to do, so I came out to the city again. I spent the whole day going back and forth from Jongno 1-ga to 3-ga. I spent the day looking up at the signs hanging high in the forest of countless buildings.

After eating a bowl of jajangmyeon for lunch and dinner, all I needed now was a room in a cheap inn. The dim electric light, the stained blanket, the musty smell of the Seoul inn—even while thinking the lodging fee was expensive, I waited for the night to pass without being able to fall asleep right away.

After passing one evening like this, in the early morning, I went to Moraenae, which I had been to once yesterday, without asking for directions.

The sunlight rising on the distant mountain gradually illuminates the world. When I visited Mr. Gu Jwa-seok's house, he, who was wondering about the visitor who had come yesterday as he had heard from his wife, confirmed my face and, feeling it was unexpected, had me come into the room.

As the conversation went back and forth, a greater sense of intimacy and trust develops between the two people. When I saw his wife coming into the room with a table set for my breakfast, I felt sorry for having come to see him so early.

We had a conversation after clearing the table. I talked about my aspirations, and he hurried to prepare to go out with me.

In the time I spent with him, I could feel that he was a young man with a strong conviction, even while living a difficult life.

The two people who got off the bus at Jongno 2-ga were about to meet some well-known politicians.

At that time, in the building of the Judicial Scriveners' Association in Jongno, there was the office of the founding preparatory committee of the Gukmin Party. It was because my brother Gu Jwa-seok had said he would introduce me to Mr. Jang Jun-ha, who was a member of the National Assembly at the time.

When we were about to get off the elevator of the building where the preparatory committee for the founding of the Gukmin Party was located, we met Mr. Jang Jun-ha, who was trying to get on that elevator.

My brother Gu Jwa-seok, pretending to know him, greeted him. Mr. Jang Jun-ha and his entourage busily entered the elevator. The signal of the elevator immediately indicates that it is going down.

I intuitively knew that the person I wanted to meet was not him. So we also took the elevator that came up next. The second place we went to was the office of Assemblyman Seo Min-ho.

14. Joining a Political Party

I entered the office of a political party with a sign for the Daejung Party attached to it, which I found on the main street of Jongno 2-ga.

My brother Gu Jwa-seok, without hesitation, knocks on the door with the sign that says "Party Leader's Office." A person who seems to be a secretary asks the two people he is seeing for the first time about their business. My brother Gu Jwa-seok speaks for me about all the circumstances.

For the first time, I was able to meet Assemblyman Seo Min-ho, an old politician who was known as a man of great character. My brother Gu Jwa-seok, as if he knew him from before, asked the teacher, who was the representative of that political party and a member of the National Assembly, about his own story and his recent well-being, and the teacher also easily answered my brother Gu Jwa-seok's words.

In particular, he shows a lot of interest in me, whom he is seeing for the first time. The teacher also called Mr. Jang Jae-cheol, who was his former secretary and the director of the party's organization bureau, and introduced me to him.

There, I was introduced to young youth leaders by Mr. Jang Jae-cheol. While I was with the young people who gathered there, I felt a lot of empathy and intimacy. I also wanted to associate with them and find a good common purpose.

Only then did I feel that it was a good thing that I had come to Seoul. On that day, I submitted my application to join the Daejung Party in front of everyone, and I felt as if I had become a famous politician in that instant.

So I even made an oath to God.

"From now on, I will devote my body and mind to the glory of the nation rather than my own happiness."

I felt that I could never forget that moment. I thought about how I could become the most excellent patriot in this country.

The people I didn't know until just a moment ago, upon the fact that I had signed the application for party membership, the people who were there treated me without any reservation, as if we had been friends for 10 years, even though we had just met.

I heard many stories that I was curious about during the short time I was with the young party officials there. And when I was leaving the office, everyone shook my hand and even received greetings wishing me good luck.

The streets of Jongno at noon were filled with people. Thinking of my brother Gu Jwa-seok, who had taken the time to guide me for my sake, when I would have skipped lunch if I were alone, we stopped by a haejangguk (hangover soup) restaurant in Cheongjin-dong, ordered a bottle of soju and haejangguk, and had lunch.

As men's hearts connected, we feel a friendship like we've known each other for a hundred years.

The two people who went out on the street took a car in the direction of Seoul Station. Seoul Station plaza was bustling with people wanting to leave for the provinces and people coming into Seoul.

I got a ticket for the regular express train to Busan, and my brother Gu Jwa-seok saw me off and said goodbye.

As an unseated passenger in the crowded space of the passenger car, I, who was lucky enough to just get my butt on a seat, opened my chest wide while looking out the passing window.

In my head, I heard the voices of people I didn't know. The sound of the iron wheels of the train running on the rails felt like a cry of youth pounding in my chest.

As the night deepened, the train arrived at Busan Station, and from the next day, I began to gather comrades one by one to found a local party chapter.

My start, without any prestige, money, or power, was, in a word, a path of hardship.

Thinking that a baby grows up to be an adult, I thought that someday these things would also mature.

One day, I called about 10 simple people who were tired of life to a small room in my sister's house where I was staying.

With a bottle of soju and a pitcher of makgeolli placed in the middle of the room, I had the people sit around it and rushed the formal procedures for founding a local party chapter.

As the alcohol they offered each other circulated through their bodies, no one raised any objections. The people who had gathered, because they knew me, were just surprised by my behavior.

From this time on, I had the people take out their resident registration cards and, while making the necessary documents for the founding preparatory committee, I got their seals.

The people who knew that what I was doing was not a bad thing were relieved. Most of the people, when I said I was forming a political party, took a cooperative attitude, perhaps hoping to get some kind of connection.

Without much difficulty, I submitted the documents I had made to the political party division of the election management committee and received a receipt.

I then hurried the remaining formal procedures. I went to the post office, made a long-distance call to the director of the Daejung Party's central party organization, and also sent a telegram to my brother Gu Jwa-seok.

These were things that had been promised with the people there when I parted in Seoul. Everything went smoothly.

I also received the date for the founding convention, and reserved a room in a wedding hall to be used as the convention hall on the scheduled date.

The Daejung Party's central party sent down the secretary-general to the local party chapter's founding convention, and my brother Gu Jwa-seok attended from Seoul to Busan.

In the quite spacious wedding hall, the seats were filled with poor villagers, friends, and the laborers who had cooperated in my founding convention.

The convention proceeds with the order of events following the planned schedule. I, for the first time in my life, looked at the familiar faces of only the villagers as if they were an audience and began my speech.

Since I had not prepared and memorized the text in advance, I felt a slight sense of burden due to my slightly pounding heart and flushed face.

As when I went out as a speaker at a speech contest, I looked at the audience and bowed.

When one person clapped, others followed suit. A cool breeze came in through the open window. Looking at the people sitting in their seats, I slowly opened my mouth.

"To all the fellow party members who have worked hard to create this event today! And to the distinguished guests who have made this event even more brilliant!

At this moment, I am at a loss for what words of greeting to offer for you and for myself.

I would like to ask you to lead and whip me in the future so that I may become a useful person in this land.

The clear words I can promise you here today through this event are that I will work even harder at whatever I do, and if there is anything I wish for, it is that I, who have lived through hardship and adversity, will strive to make myself known to you and your friends through my future work.

I am a person who believes in the will of God and believes in the truth. If there is anything we lack today, it is only that the past was wrong.

If something was wrong, it must be corrected in the future. These things have been thought of as very difficult, but they are very easy depending on the person who does them.

A hypocrite and a liar will not achieve it in their lifetime, but anyone can do it through action and practice.

Among those who have life on this earth, few do not wish for happiness. However, it is the way of the world that those who are willing to fight against injustice for the sake of their generation are not common.

Is this the will of God? What is clear is that for a blessed society, for a blessed nation, one must stand on the side where truth prevails, which is my conviction. With this conviction alone, I will substitute my greetings. Thank you."

When my greeting was over, the people applauded vigorously.

They all looked surprised. The program continued without a break.

Mr. Lee Mong, who was the secretary-general of the central party, explained that Mr. Seo Min-ho, the leader of the Daejung Party, could not attend this convention due to a busy schedule and gave a congratulatory speech. Following three cheers, the founding convention came to an end.

The people who had gathered scattered. Even though a difficult road lay ahead, I was just happy.

When I think of the time I threw my opponent in the ssireum (Korean wrestling) ring, I also felt a sense of pride.

And so, with only conscience and my own courage, in a situation where I was struggling to even make a living for myself, I became interested in the problems of the nation, society, and the people, and created my own time to feel anger and sorrow in reality.

Human existence is strictly divided into dreams and reality. It was my life that no one tried to take responsibility for.

Like anyone else, I was bound to have principles of life. To fill a hungry stomach, I had to labor, not struggle. The reason I could not refuse even difficult or dangerous work was because there were circumstances in reality that I could not avoid.

I had to carry steel plates on my shoulder at the shipyard and carry them to distant places, and there were times when sparks from the welding machine would fly and touch my skin, but I couldn't even shake them off.

But I felt that the things I was doing were out of affection for the future rather than pain, so I comforted myself in that way. Time changed the seasons and a year passed.

I would sometimes experience unfair things, but the unbearable pain was when good people suffered as victims under power.

In my heart, which was looking at the world, futility and emptiness piled up, and I had to face sleepless nights because of my thoughts.

I began to feel the sorrow of the nation.

Parents raise their children, wanting them to grow up strong and brave, but the kings of this land, the rulers of today, know how to love their own children but not the children of others.

Is it that they are determined to uphold the tyranny of the dynasty and the tradition of absolute obedience, which has not produced a single decent hero in thousands of years of history, by unconditionally suppressing people and annihilating the most noble courage and individual struggle of human beings?

In this sad emotion of falling into such sorrow, I asked my heart what my mission was. It was then that I, in the midst of my anguish, first decided what I had to do.

It was the winter when it had been half a year since I joined the political party and became a party member. I decided to organize a lecture to inform my compatriots, who were living, forgetting reality, that a terrible future was approaching. I named it the Democratic Citizens' Unity Rally.

15. The First Sense of Mission

I frequented various post offices and often applied for long-distance calls to Seoul. For the first time, unable to suppress my sense of justice in reality, I single-handedly hurried to prepare for a citizens' unity rally to restore the rights of the people.

I called the headquarters of the Daejung Party and asked them to send some speakers. After several phone calls, I received a list of attendees, and the problems of my decision turning into action remained.

First, I had to reserve a venue. It was a place that could hold about 2,000 people. I even prepared 600 chairs. I also asked a printing shop to make posters to put on the walls and formed a preparation committee of about 30 members.

As it was something I had no experience with and was organizing on my own, many mistakes were made. I had to set up all the proceedings and plans by myself, and the entire preparation committee was composed of only neighborhood ruffians or construction site laborers.

On the days they all gathered in one place, they each had different opinions, which led to arguments. The situation was such that I had to appease them to proceed with the work, as it seemed I would be exhausted right next to them.

Sometimes, when alcohol entered their bodies, they would make a fuss about seniority. On some days, they would show their enthusiasm, bowing and scraping, saying they were being loyal to me.

But then there were times when they would not try to understand others at all. As they themselves said, they followed me out of a sense of loyalty.

Three days before the rally, the posters I had ordered from the printing shop came out. In a small room in my sister's house, I bought white paper and had a barber, who was said to be good at Chinese characters in the village, write the arch for the rally venue, and I had my ruffian friends with glue pots put up promotional posters at conspicuous intersections.

Now, the only problem left was whether an audience would gather. The only place to ask about this feeling was the preparation committee members, who were composed of ruffians and laborers who were around me.

All 30 of them said the same thing, that there was no problem, but I couldn't be relieved. The speakers from Seoul are scheduled to come down tomorrow afternoon for the rally in the morning, believing only my words. The leader of the Daejung Party, Seo Min-ho, also said he would come down.

To show even a little respect to the speakers coming here, I asked the laborers and village ruffians who were the rally preparation committee members to gather at the 4th-floor auditorium of the Yeongdo Wedding Hall, which would be used as the rally venue, two hours before the train arrival time, and after feeding each of them a glass of soju late at night, I sent them back.

I couldn't sleep because of the tension. The next day, at the promised time of two in the afternoon, 36 familiar people gathered in the auditorium that would be used as the rally venue. Out of an abundance of caution, I greeted them, asking them to do well.

They all answered coolly, saying that if I just trusted them, it would be fine. At that time, I really wanted to believe their actions.

We split up and took several city buses and departed for the station. There was still a considerable amount of time left until the train time.

At first, the group, which had been cautious, became anxious, perhaps because there was still an hour left. One by one, they disappeared. A sense of failure arose in my heart.

Through the remaining people, I had them find the people who had disappeared, and they gave the shabby excuse that it was because of the cold, and they smelled of alcohol.

In about thirty minutes, it was time for the train to arrive, but two people from the group were bickering.

One person looked at me and said he was going back. Another person also said he was going back. Now, the situation was that everyone was murmuring. While I was appeasing them, I was the only one whose heart was breaking and whose hands were getting sweaty.

Just then, a welcome sound came from the speaker. An announcement came that the tourist train would arrive soon.

Thanks to the announcement, even those who had been fighting and talking about leaving shut their mouths. I got 37 admission tickets and everyone went into the station.

Imagining a scene from a movie, I lined them up at intervals on the platform where the train would arrive.

At the scheduled time, the express train enters the station. People get off, and familiar faces get off the train.

I ran over there. That side also recognized my greeting and looked happy.

I hung the flower necklace, which I had bought for a discount after much haggling in the morning, around the neck of Seo Min-ho, the leader of the Daejung Party, and took his outstretched hand. I shouted "Manse!" (Long live!) in front of the people who had come out to greet me. Everyone followed my lead and shouted "Manse!" in unison.

The station was in an uproar, and the people who got off the train late turned around with surprised expressions. I told the people who were following me out of curiosity, wondering if something had happened, to take the bus and go home, and I promised to meet a few people.

My heart couldn't put the people who had come down from Seoul on the bus, so I chartered a commercial passenger car that was waiting in the station plaza to Yeongdo.

When I asked where I should book a hotel, the party leader, Seo Min-ho, said that if there was a suitable place in Yeongdo, we should book it near the wedding hall. I happened to think of a place, so I booked two rooms at a place called Dongwon Yeogwan, located on the edge of Yeongdo Market.

Somehow, the people who had been at the station gathered in front of the inn one by one. I, feeling grateful that they had saved my face today, had them greet the speakers who had come down from the party one by one and also introduced them.

After sending them off like that, after a while, I see a shadow loitering in the alley in front of the inn. It's clear that they, who have no money, have been drinking somewhere, and they are making a racket in the alley until it's curfew time.

One of them even threatens me, saying, "Let's see what happens tomorrow." I was the one who was starting to get anxious about this.

It was after twelve o'clock when I returned to my sister's house, my lodging, and tried to sleep. This thought and that thought made my head even more alert.

In the end, I got up from my seat, unable to fall asleep, worried about what would happen today, at the sound of the church bell. My head, which had been fine until a moment ago, started to ache, and an unbearable fatigue came over me.

I picked up my clothes and ran out into the cold street.

The first thing I had to do was go to the radio shop that rented out microphones next to the streetcar terminus.

I went to a house that hadn't even opened its doors and woke up the sleeping owner. I asked him to install a few microphones in the wedding hall by eight o'clock. I went around to the houses of the party officials, woke up the sleeping people, hurried to install the arch for the rally venue, and also had to worry about mobilizing the audience.

Around eight o'clock, a person from the radio shop came and installed a microphone at the rally venue. Three or four party officials were decorating the wedding hall.

As time flowed, I became more and more anxious. After nine o'clock in the morning, I became flustered and started to rush around without looking back and forth.

No one is confident about the audience. Until then, not a single face of the ruffians and laborers who were the preparation committee members was visible. With a heartbroken feeling, I grabbed the microphone that had finished being installed.

Now I was about to send out an announcement to as far as the microphone sound would carry.

I brought the microphone close to my mouth. Just as I was about to part my lips, someone handed me a business card and told me a guest had arrived. It was my comrade Lee Kyung-sik, the party's propaganda director, with whom I had a close connection even though we had only had a little contact.

With a happy heart, I ran towards the nearby barbershop where he was said to be. In the barbershop, comrade Lee Kyung-sik, with his face covered in soap, was getting a shave, and when he confirmed my face reflected in the mirror, he gave his characteristic smile. I urged the barber to hurry up and finish the shave.

I brought him, who had been exhausted from a whole night of train travel, into the rally venue without even feeding him breakfast, and confirming the time pointed to by the clock's hand at 9:30.

Looking at the vast empty space and the empty chairs, I was worried that it was impossible to mobilize an audience.

I forcibly handed the microphone to comrade Lee Kyung-sik, who was standing next to me, and asked him to make an announcement.

He, looking at my behavior, was not more flustered than me.

He said he had come down on his own, borrowing travel expenses, worried about the rally venue that seemed to have been rushed without my plan, and forgetting his hunger, he grabbed the microphone and brought it close to his mouth and opened his mouth.

"Bird, bird, bluebird, do not sit on the mung bean flower, if the mung bean flower falls, the mung bean jelly seller will cry and go.

This is ○○○. From 10 a.m. today, a grand lecture will be held here where the sound of the microphone is spreading, with Mr. Wolpa Seo Min-ho, who has fought against injustice, illegality, and corruption, and other courageous people who fight against dictatorship. We would like to inform all citizens to gather here where the sound of the microphone is going out.

Bird, bird, bluebird, do not sit on the mung bean flower, if the mung bean flower falls, the mung bean jelly seller will cry and go. This is ○○○."

The announcement continued to flow from the microphone. Did the mournful voice, tinged with pathos, begin to have an effect?

For the first time, people were seen entering the wedding hall.

With a happy heart, I turned my head towards the people coming in. They are familiar faces. The group was plainclothes police officers.

They look around the empty rally hall and make an embarrassed face. And yet, one by one, they checked the faces of the people who were there.

I start to have an ominous feeling. I was worried that something might go wrong.

In the meantime, it became 10 o'clock, and one by one, the audience began to enter. The entrance was immediately lined up. The seats were filled, and the aisle was filled with people.

The announcement was changing to a hall-organizing announcement.

By the time a sigh of relief burst from my chest, I quickly ran to the inn.

I greeted the group that had come down from Seoul in the morning and informed them that all preparations were ready.

They ask if people have gathered at the rally venue, whether out of concern or comfort. I reported the current situation as a great success.

At 10:20, according to the emcee's clear voice, the program continued.

"In a situation where our history feels like it is going wrong due to the royal authority and dictators, and in these days when people are reluctant to appear in the right place, comrade Lee Sam-han here, with a patriotic love to seek courage and wisdom in this land, has created this venue today to achieve the unity of all citizens and the will to protect democratic civil rights. First, I ask for a round of applause of encouragement for comrade Lee Sam-han."

At the emcee's introduction, a long round of applause followed from the hall.

I raised my hand and walked towards the podium.

As the audience's eyes were fixed on me, I also sent my gaze towards the faces in the audience, one by one. The hall becomes as quiet as if water had been poured over it.

I feel a sense of bewilderment in my heart. I feel my body and mind have become tired.

Until just a moment ago, I had forgotten to prepare the most important opening address, having been pushed by the things I had to do.

It was impossible to just stand there where the audience was watching. No matter how hard I wracked my brain, no good idea came to mind right away.

The hall begins to stir again with a buzzing atmosphere.

I tried to find all the words I knew in my head and opened my mouth.
