In the afternoon, even if I was careful, I would get drunk along with the actions of persistent people.

The task of handing out the rest of the invitations could be postponed to the next day, but the difficult thought that arose the next day was that time would not wait for my circumstances and would just pass by.

When I counted the number of wedding invitations I handed out while running around for 3 days, it was only about 70.

Finally, the day of my wedding arrived, tomorrow.

My situation now was that I had to hurry with the tasks of grooming myself into the shape of a new groom. I had to take a bath and get a haircut.

As the wedding time drew near, worries began to pile up. When I thought about how empty it would be if there were no guests in the large wedding hall, a feeling of shame even arose. No matter how much I wracked my brain, no particular method came to mind. I thought there was nothing I could do now.

My anxious heart turned to pain, and if it was something I had to face no matter what, I could only hope that time would pass a little more quickly.

It became that kind of midday. Even though it was a day before, about 10 comrades who lived in Seoul heard the news that I was getting married and came down to see the wedding. Also, from my hometown, my siblings and cousins came to my older brother's house.

On May 13, 1973, at noon in the 3rd-floor special hall of Jeil Wedding Hall, a miracle that could not have been imagined happened to the 400 or so seats. The seats were filled with congratulatory guests and even the aisles were packed.

Many people came from the bride's side as well, but hundreds of people who had not received an invitation came after hearing the news of my marriage. The clear voice of the master of ceremonies conducting the program held the busy footsteps of the congratulatory guests.

Applause and laughter continuously broke out, and the roughly 600 guests were fascinated by the atmosphere of the hall, and there was even a case where people who were intending to attend another event stayed to watch, making the entrance of the hall completely packed.

The congratulatory address by the secretary-general of the Daejung Party began with the opening words, "To the distinguished guests who have participated in the wedding of the most outstanding young man in this country," captivating the hearts of the guests, and the sight of the groom adorned in a new suit was, for that moment, an object of envy in people's eyes.

The wedding ceremony went on for a considerable amount of time and then ended. Finally, after finishing the presentation of the wedding gifts in front of the relatives, all that was left was for the bride and me to set off on our honeymoon trip.

The bank where the bride worked, having considered our situation, provided a passenger car, and the groom's groomsman and the bride's bridesmaid came along to fill the time.

The car carrying us drove the group around places like Tongdosa Temple in Yangsan and Haeundae until it was time to take the plane to Jeju, our final honeymoon destination, and at appropriate places, it stopped the car to let us take commemorative photos.

It was nearly 6 p.m. when the people who were with us at Suyeong Airfield, the plane's departure point, had just the bride and me get on the plane and then went back.

The plane takes off with a roar into the summer sky where the evening glow is about to set.

Looking down from inside the plane through the window, the sea and islands were visible from time to time. Until then, the fact that I had become a groom did not feel real, just like a story from a fairy tale book. Looking at the face of the bride sitting in the next seat, all sorts of thoughts arise.

Thinking of my own situation, I thought that if this woman were to try to learn the way of a wife, she would have to endure quite a bit of hardship. A sorry feeling arose, as if I had committed a terrible act.

Another thought was of my parents who passed away when I was young. The thought of how proud and happy they would have been to see me like this today if they were still alive leaves a lingering feeling of regret in a corner of my heart.

Not even 30 minutes had passed since the plane was in the sky when the voice of a female flight attendant on the plane came out from the speaker. She announced that we would be landing at our destination shortly, so the passengers should fasten their seatbelts.

Looking down at the land on the sea that appeared below the window, I think that we have now come all the way to Jeju.

Reaching a thousand-li road in an instant felt like hearing a story from a new world. The two of us, having arrived in Jeju faster than expected, came out of the airport exit, and someone in the darkening surroundings acted as if they knew us.

At the airport exit, a friend of the bride from her high school days was there to meet the two of us with the man who was her husband. Through the women's introduction, I exchanged greetings with the man I met in this unfamiliar place.

The man, Mr. Park, put our group of four into a taxi and guided us somewhere. As the women talked so affectionately in the car, the awkwardness between the men disappears.

Where the taxi stopped, I looked up and a sign that read Yonggung Hoe-jip (Dragon Palace Raw Fish House) caught my eye. Mr. Park guided our group there and kept ordering food that the four of us could eat to our hearts' content.

I felt the burden of being too much in his debt on our first meeting, but as the glasses of alcohol that the men offered each other were emptied, the uncomfortable feeling also disappeared.

The atmosphere of the table naturally flowed to the topic of the honeymoon. The two of them taught us some helpful words for the time we would spend in Jeju.

Also, worrying about the night the two of us would spend tonight, they said that perhaps because it was the honeymoon season, it was difficult to find a room at the KAL Hotel, the only one in Jeju City. We had not yet decided on our lodging.

After the meal was over, the two of them, for us who had come to Jeju for the first time, guided us to a place they knew so that we could arrange our lodging. They also told us tips for the next day's schedule, the honeymoon tour, and then said their goodbyes.

The room that just the two of us entered was to be the place for our first wedding night. It was not just because the bride seemed exceptionally amiable, but perhaps because of the single reason that we were married, in my heart she felt like a comrade I had known for a long time.

The first feeling I felt was a new one that welled up, "So this is what happens between a man and a woman who have held a wedding ceremony?"

I felt like I was being treated like a human being for the first time since I was born in the world. The bride and I thought about the next day's travel plan.

Remembering the story Mr. and Mrs. Park had told us, I used the phone in the room to ask the operator and made a reservation with a taxi company. It was promised that we would charter a taxi for the whole day for 8,000 won and that a car would be sent in front of our lodging early in the morning.

The two of us had nothing left to do. Only then did the tension ease. The two people who had gone to bed heard the phone bell in their weary consciousness.

When I opened my eyes, it was morning and the day had broken.

At the entrance of the lodging, the reserved taxi had arrived. The two of us, without even having breakfast, hurriedly got up from our spots and rushed. The bride fixed her makeup, and I changed my clothes and urged the bride with my expression.

When the two of us came out to the entrance holding our bags, an employee of the lodging establishment recognized us and introduced us to the taxi driver. The chartered car's driver, who still looked young, skillfully took the luggage and guided the two of us toward the taxi.

The car slowly began to exit Jeju City. It seems the taxis there have been used more for charter by honeymooners than as a means of transportation for the local people, as the driver skillfully drove the car and asked about our lodging for the evening.

When I told him we would be staying at the Ilchul Hotel in Seongsanpo, which Mr. and Mrs. Park had told us about yesterday, the driver explained the travel course schedule and, since we didn't know the geography of Jeju, the car's driver told us stories about various places.

The first place he stopped at while driving was under a 500-year-old pine tree by the side of the road on the outskirts of Jeju City.

The driver, taking the camera we were carrying, made us pose like a professional photographer and then pressed the shutter.

Passing through Samseonghyeol, a legendary historic site of Jeju, the car entered the 5·16 Road and moved slowly along the road that crosses Hallasan Mountain. The scenery of Hallasan, just then lush with green, came into view.

The taxi driver would stop the car at any place worth seeing on the planned course and, no less than any tour guide, explained its history without his words getting stuck.

When it was lunchtime, the car entered Seogwipo City. I saw a restaurant sign on the roadside and had him stop the car, and the three of us ordered a meal together. For the bride and me, it was breakfast and lunch combined.

The grains of rice were coarse and got stuck in my throat. The taste of the food at the small restaurant did not suit my palate.

The chartered car again took the two of us and drove to a place where there were three waterfalls. No matter how slowly we looked around, the time was not yet three in the afternoon.

The car's driver asked a question. To us, who were unfamiliar with Jeju for the first time, he asked what we would do from now on. The course to the hotel was finished.

When the taxi dropped the two of us off in front of the entrance, the employees who were there ran out and took our luggage. I paid the charter fee to the driver who had brought the two of us and had the car turn back.

The employee at the front desk, leaving me who was asking what kind of rooms were available, advertised the guest rooms and watched my expression to see what kind of room I would choose.

I designated a Korean-style room on the upper side of the entrance, which had the cheapest rate. A female employee in charge of guest rooms immediately moved the luggage. The two of us followed that guide up to the second-floor room.

As I entered the hotel room, anxious thoughts came to mind. It was the concern of how long the cash I had on hand would be able to sustain our time.

I asked the bride how much money was left on hand. She hinted that about 10,000 won was left. The tangerine boxes, which we had bought at a tangerine grove as a supposed gift out of money that had no surplus, put the two of us in a difficult position.

A sense of failure arises in my head as I do the math. The hotel room bill to be paid, the food expenses for the two of us for today and tomorrow, and also the transportation fare to get to Jeju City kept burdening my mind.

It is quite bewildering that I came on a honeymoon to an island without calculating these things. There was still time left before the sun would set. I got up from my seat.

The bride and I walked toward the seaside of Seongsanpo, a small port. On the country road, not a single spot of which was paved with asphalt, there was nothing to see other than women passing by carrying something like a mesh bag.

When I thought we had walked for a while, a warehouse appeared by the water. There was nowhere else to go, so we had to turn back after reaching the place where the road was blocked. The fishy smell of raw fish wafted to my nose.

I moved my feet, turning my eyes toward the country village with a slower gait than when I had come. The hands of the watch I received as a wedding gift pointed to 6 p.m. I looked around this alley and that alley of the narrow village street.

People on the roadside glance at me and the bride, who is finely made up and stylishly dressed. Even after wandering around for a good while, the house I wanted to find was not in sight.

Thinking that there couldn't possibly not be such a house in a village where people live, I wandered around unfamiliar streets several times, yet when I saw people, I couldn't even ask.

After wandering for a long time, on one side of the road, a paint-peeled sign that read "Junghwa Banjeom" (Chinese Restaurant) came into view. I pulled the bride's hand and entered the place with her.

The interior facilities of the restaurant in the small village were shabby. Streaks of grime flowed down on all sides. The bride and I sat facing each other at a shabby table.

After quite a while, a man who seemed to be both the cook and the owner came to the table where the two of us were sitting, poured water into barley tea cups, and took our order, asking what we would like to order.

I examined the menus written on a cloth between the kitchen and the hall, which was terribly covered in dust. The names of Korean and Chinese dishes were written messily and in large numbers.

I thought it was rather fortunate that there were no other customers. I glanced at the bride's expression. And yet, I asked the person trying to take our order if everything on the menu was available. The other person replied that it was.

For the bride's share, I ordered yukgaejang, and for my share, I ordered a large portion of jjajangmyeon, which was 200 won cheaper than that.

The bride was startled and asked in a fluster why I didn't order yukgaejang as well, but I had no excuse to make, so I made up an outlandish lie that I usually like flour-based food.

The food came out after making the two of us wait for a long time. There was a difference in taste from the food in the city. And yet, the countryside jjajangmyeon, which was my second meal in a day, felt like a delicacy to me.

The bride, perhaps also a bit hungry, did not leave any yukgaejang and emptied her bowl. Only after the meal was over did I feel a bit of stability. Now the worries of today were lessened.

The two of us, who had left the restaurant, saw the evening glow of Jeju Island and returned to the hotel. On the lawn in front of the hotel, people on their honeymoons and people who came for sightseeing were sitting here and there on the grass, talking, and some were singing songs.

We also found a suitable spot in front of the hotel and began to take in the surrounding scenery. Ranch horses, without bridles, peacefully grazing on grass appeared in sight. Darkness, now chased by time, covered the surroundings. One by one, the people who were out went into the hotel.

I got up first and took the hand of the seated bride to help her up. We walked toward the hotel guest rooms. We didn't say much. And yet, it seemed our actions and intentions communicated well.

The two of us woke up at the same time even though we were not woken up at dawn. The surroundings were still dark. We washed our faces and put on our clothes. And then we came out of the hotel entrance.

On the steep path of Ilchulbong, right next to the hotel, a procession of people was already continuing. The guide booklet inside the hotel introduced Ilchulbong as a miniature version of Hallasan.

We followed the path and went up to the summit.

All around became bright, and the eastern sea burned red. A round sun slowly rose from within the sea. The gazes of the other people who had climbed to the summit were also gathered on the sea and the place of the sunrise.

The sun gradually rose into the sky, and the eastern sea, which seemed to be boiling red, began to regain its original blue color.

In a place like a crater, sunken like a gourd at the summit, I don't know if it was by someone's hand or just nature, but green grass was well harmonized, and herds of mountain goats were seen grazing here and there.

After we had seen everything there was to see, we had to go back down the steep path that was difficult to climb.

Intending to at least get the bride some breakfast, the two of us entered the restaurant inside the hotel. Inside the restaurant, I noticed people who had arrived earlier sitting here and there at tables, having their meals. I carefully looked at the menu placed on the table.

Spicy fish stew 1,000 won, grilled croaker 2,000 won, the price display on the menu gave me an immediate shock. I quickly put the menu back in its place and, telling the bride to get up, I rushed back to our room from the restaurant as if fleeing.

In the end, without even eating breakfast, I ended up just confirming the expensive hotel food prices. When things got to this point, I hated everything, the honeymoon and all.

Empty taxis frequently came into the hotel entrance. At such times, I ran to the entrance. I bargained with the taxi drivers, asking how much they would charge for two people to Jeju.

On the condition of sharing the ride, we packed our luggage and loaded ourselves into the car with the bride to Jeju City for 2,000 won, 1,000 won per person. The driver, in a small car taxi (Brisa), packed five people in like baggage to share the ride and then drove at a severe speed.

Now, in the hands of the two of us, after deducting the car fare, there is only 2,500 won left. I had never imagined that a honeymoon would be this difficult.

My head was filled with the thought of returning to Busan safely with the bride. In the speeding car, even when another ride-sharing passenger warned the driver to drive a little slower, not a single word came out of my mouth.

The car reached Jeju City faster than imagined. We got off in front of the KAL Jeju office in the city.

After ordering and eating two bowls of seolleongtang at a nearby restaurant, my heart wanted to leave Jeju quickly.

The bride, as if knowing my mind first, went into the KAL Jeju office and came out, then told me the conversation she had with the office staff there. She said that it was impossible to exchange the tickets for the 2 o'clock plane.

I had our evening flight tickets exchanged for the 12 o'clock flight tickets, which was a short while later. They said the office bus would run to the airport at 11:30 a.m., so there were only about 20 minutes left to wait. The two of us waited in the office's waiting room.

In the meantime, the bride called the friend she had met the day before yesterday to say she was leaving. 20 minutes passed quickly. The airport bus was waiting for people with its engine running.

When we arrived at the airport, the bride's friends who had first met us in Jeju were there to see us off again. I was extremely grateful for their actions.

We didn't have much time to hold on to them and talk. It was because the announcements kept urging passengers to board. I shook hands with the bride's friend's husband and went up to the plane. Perhaps because it was time, the plane immediately began to rise into the sky.

Below my feet, islands and the sea appeared. The sea viewed from the sky looked like a happy landscape. Small islands appear and pass by below. At that time, clouds were gathering in the sky.

As the plane entered the clouds, it began to shake. I was anxious with ominous thoughts, wondering if the plane might crash. A change in weather was occurring. (For 4 days after that time, planes did not fly between the mainland and Jeju.) Then, the mainland began to be visible.

The plane stopped its turbulence and took its course in a calm state. After a while, as our plane landed at Suyeong Airport, the stuffy and anxious feelings from a moment ago began to subside.

Now, a new worry began to fill my head again, about where to take the bride to sleep. No matter how much I tilted my head and thought, no brilliant method came to mind.

I asked a destitute question to the bride next to me. I asked if she had any severance pay she had received. The bride flatly denies it. Only then did I feel what a resourceless and servile man I was, and I felt ashamed.

We first stopped by my in-laws' house in Dongnae, which was close to Suyeong, and my mother-in-law and father-in-law welcomed us very much.

Receiving the food table they had hurriedly prepared, I ate my fill for the first time since getting married and went to my older brother's house to pay my respects. The people of that house, for that day only, welcomed my wife and me without making us uncomfortable.

I was planning to visit my parents' ancestral graves in my hometown with the bride the next day.

That night, because of a frustrating and worrying matter, I asked my older brother something I was curious about.

When I asked how much congratulatory money had come in, my brother said about 200,000 won had come in, and while making a poor excuse, his expression changed as he said he had spent it all, without telling me where he had spent the money. Now, even the last hope I had clung to disappears.

As someone who knew my brother's usual behavior, I knew there was no gain in arguing about this. I feel how heartless the world's sentiment can be. Still, I thought that my brother, who couldn't even contribute a penny in condolence money, would at least keep the congratulatory money and give it back, but now even that thought was shattered.

I realized the truth in the saying that when one is in such a situation, the heart actually becomes firmer. What use is there in crying alone in a world where no one sympathizes? There was no place to even lament my erased fate.

Thinking of my pathetic self, I couldn't sleep, my heart burning as I waited for tomorrow. And the next day, I took my bride and visited my hometown after a long time.

The very first thing we did was go before the graves of my two parents who had left me, a young child, behind and passed away, and the bride and I bowed.

When we burned the clothes the bride had prepared by the graveside and sprinkled the alcohol we had prepared on the grass of the graves, my nose immediately tingled and tears formed in my eyes.

'Mother, Father, I have gotten married. My bride is also next to me.'

In my heart, these words kept trying to come out, but I couldn't utter a single word out loud.

My sister and relatives who lived in my hometown greeted me, a married man, with very happy faces for that moment, but not a single person asked how I would live from now on. They were all people who were just being observant, so it seemed they were avoiding painful words.

We stayed up all night and returned to Busan. The things I was worried about became more and more urgent. Inside, I was becoming anxious.

I resented my brother, thinking that if I had just taken care of the congratulatory money on the wedding day, I could have gotten a 'sakwolse' room, but it was all in the past now. At times like these, I grew to hate myself.

Having no other choice, I told my wife to go to her parents' house for just about 3 days and sent her off, and I myself roamed the city like a madman. I, who am not thick-skinned, could not bring up the topic of money to anyone.

A day passed. The god of fate did not just turn a blind eye to me. Coincidentally, a stroke of luck occurred. 200,000 won came into my possession. That evening, I rushed to my in-laws' house in Dongnae.

My wife, who had been waiting anxiously thinking about my situation, showed a very happy expression upon seeing my face when I visited. That evening, we spent one night together at her parents' house and went around looking for a room.

My immediate thought was that I wanted to live a little far away from my older brother. My wife made a regretful suggestion to find a room near her parents' house in Dongnae, but I intended to find a room near Seomyeon.

Even after combing through the nearby neighborhoods of the Seomyeon area all day, there was no suitable room for 200,000 won. Just then, hearing that a room had become available at the Min family's house in my older sister's neighborhood, we secured a single room that was not equipped with a kitchen for a deposit of 200,000 won and a monthly rent of 5,000 won, and set up our newlywed life.

After arranging the things my wife brought in the room, there was just enough space left for two people to lie down.

When it was mealtime, my wife brought in a lavish table she had prepared with care. Looking at the food table, I felt the reward of being married for the first time, but I also felt like I was shouldering another person's burden when I was struggling to live alone.

For the sake of our life together, I could not just choose an easy path for myself. I went around looking for any kind of work that would bring in an income.

As I had lived through hardship, adapting to a difficult environment did not require training. When I earned money, a penny or two, I entrusted it to my wife for safekeeping.

Then one day it was. Feeling a bit tired, I went home in the middle of the day with the thought of wanting to go in and rest. My wife was sitting in the room with the neighborhood children.

My wife, too, for our life together, had gathered the elementary school students of the neighborhood and was tutoring them. Seeing that, my heart was immediately touched and I felt a new emotion. Changing my mind from wanting to rest, I came back outside.

As the newlywed life grew longer, the life that had been only anxious gradually improved through the efforts of the two of us. On the one hand, the political situation was increasingly fraught with instability, and the propaganda of the Yushin, which claimed to save the nation, muzzled even the just words of the people.

Citing the Political Parties Act, they made political parties take down their signs, and through surveillance and oppression, they trampled on the aspirations of talented people.

The Daejung Party, which I had loved, could not withstand the financial pressure and the intensifying persecution from the regime, and eventually had to take down its sign.

Even if people had a conscience, there was no place to use that conscience. The era was dawning when those who possessed justice had to be beaten. Watching a world where only the cat that does not try to catch mice could succeed, I felt a sense of futility.

In such a world, hot-tempered people stepped forward, saying they would create a political party, trusting in their own affections. The interest of people who felt frustrated was focused here.

In my chest too, affection for society began to ignite in my heart. For some reason, people pointed their fingers at those seeking to engage in politics, calling them crazy.

Only an unknowable heart pained the chests of those with aspirations. A person who could speak justly received wary glares wherever they went.

I thought intently for several days about whether I should participate in the new party with my conscience at this difficult time. When I thought about political activism within my own country, which was more difficult than the independence movement under Japanese colonial rule, my wife's face tormented me.

After thinking about one woman and my homeland for several days, I eventually applied for the position of organization chief for the first electoral district of Busan, Jung-gu Yeongdo, in the Democratic Unification Party, through Mr. Park Jae-woo, who was the Busan organization chief of the new party that had hung up the sign of the Unification Party.

Thinking of the things to come, I run around here and there to contribute even a little more to the household.

About a month after that, word was passed on by a person that my organization application had passed the organizing committee and been decided. And not long after, I received a message from Mr. Park Jae-woo asking to meet once.

At that time, my personal situation was such that I couldn't get away because I had a personal matter of earning a few pennies. My heart wanted to take the train to Seoul to save this country's politics, but reality made me postpone my actions for a day or two.

Then one day, I came to read an article in the newspaper about the petition movement for constitutional amendment. I was immediately seized by an impulse. I stay up all night, forgoing sleep, and think.

The consciousness that if it can be done by petitioning for a society I want to believe in, then the law that the people can like must be liberated from the dictator, was rising hotly in my chest.

My heart began to grow warm. I could not bear it any longer. I left the work I was doing and took the train to Seoul that very day. Having come up to Seoul, I looked up at the newly built buildings and wandered through the city center for the first time in a long time.

The very first place I went to find was the headquarters of the new party, the Unification Party. Unexpectedly, I met comrade Lee Kyung-sik there. Comrade Lee Kyung-sik also introduced me to the young intellectual patriots who frequented that party. After exchanging greetings, I found that we all understood each other's intentions.

They asked me about various stories happening in Busan. And one of them hastily urged me to take on the position of the Busan chapter head of the constitutional amendment petition movement and fight together.

Hearing these words, I got up from my seat and went into the secretariat of the Unification Party. Unfamiliar people look at my face.

I walked up to the party's secretary-general. (At that time, the secretary-general of the Unification Party was Mr. Ha Ki-rak, the former dean of the College of Liberal Arts and Sciences at Kyungpook National University.)

I introduced myself and greeted him. As Secretary-General Ha's face confirmed my name, he became awkward like a man being chased. It was something I could tell right away, but due to someone's interference, a fact that had been finalized and decided had been changed to pending. My mind, having confirmed the fact, could not be at ease.

As I was about to leave the Unification Party, the young executive party members of the Unification Party with whom I had just been talking took me to a nearby coffee shop.

They wanted me to be able to do the same work with them, but I did not want to say anything about any decision here right now. Perhaps because of my behavior, the atmosphere of the table immediately becomes awkward.

Because of my heavy heart, I made the excuse that I had a place to go and got up from my seat. A corner of my heart, as I left the coffee shop, was desolate. In a word, the future expectations I had placed on the Unification Party crumble.

I was walking without having decided on a destination. Where to go. After wandering for a long time, I set my direction toward Nakwon-dong.

It was because I thought I would say hello to Chairman Kim Chul of the Unification Socialist Party, who was still enduring difficult conditions and protecting a party office more shabby than a village senior citizens' center without giving up his difficult actions, and if there was nothing else to see, I would leave Seoul.

The Unification Socialist Party office in Nakwon-dong that I entered was too quiet and desolate.

A party member who knew my face greeted me warmly. My heart is about to become sorrowful. I thought it was a tragedy that could exist in large numbers in this land. The situation of a single political party was too miserable.

Every person had a gaunt face with only their eyes glittering. Chairman Kim Chul, at my unexpected visit, put a broad smile on his face and gladly extended his hand.

Somehow he had heard, he first brought up the story of my wedding and said he was sorry he could not attend the wedding at that time. The executive party officials there surround me as the center.

The sofa in the chairman's office creaks when I lean my body against it. With a pang in my heart, the situation here immediately comes to mind. While exchanging stories, only the conversation was full of spirit for everyone.

Those who knew me confirmed my quite changed face and joked that the rice my wife made must be really good. I also laughed.

When I said that when I had one mouth, my head was heavy, but now that I have two mouths, even my shoulders are heavy, the people there burst into laughter.

Just as I was about to get up from my seat, someone grabs my hand and pulls me, saying let's have a cup of tea. Chairman Kim Chul also recommended that I do so.

I got up from my seat. I exchanged farewell handshakes with several people. The people who guided me to the coffee shop were comrade Lee Dong-yeol and comrade Baek Chul, who had served as the spokesman for the Seoul Metropolitan Democratic Restoration.

While drinking a cup of tea, I could hear from the two men about recent stories happening in Seoul.

And I was also able to hear the fact that Mr. Wolpa Seo Min-ho, whose news I had been curious about, had opened a Unification Researcher Association office across from Susong Elementary School and was in contact with many people. Having come up to Seoul, I now had one more place to visit.

The one day I spent in Seoul was very busy for me. The three people who left the coffee shop scattered in different directions.

Without much thought, my steps headed toward Cheongjin-dong. I grabbed a passerby and asked where Susong Elementary School was. The people in the Cheongjin-dong area immediately pointed out Susong Elementary School.

I was able to find the Unification Researcher Association office with a large sign at a short distance from where I was.

When I entered the office, many people were gathered, and the atmosphere itself seemed dignified. A stranger grabs me and asks what business I have. I introduced myself, saying I came to just pay my respects to the chairman.

At that time, comrade Jang Jae-cheol who was in that office recognized my face and welcomed me with a serious expression. He presses me, asking when I came up to Seoul. I explained the situation, saying I arrived in Seoul at dawn.

The surrounding gazes are drawn to my face. Comrade Jang Jae-cheol recommended that I sit on the sofa side and hinted that I should wait a moment as there was a visitor. Inside the office, some people who came to meet the teacher catch my eye.

After some time, the door to the chairman's office opened and people came out. Comrade Jang Jae-cheol went into the chairman's office and came out, then guided me to the chairman's office.

The teacher, who was 72 years old, looked healthy. I paid my respects to the teacher. The teacher had me sit down and then had someone order tea.

He asks about my recent well-being, how my newlywed life is. He made an excuse that he really meant to attend the wedding, but it was because he was in America.

I thought the teacher would be busy, so I stayed for a short while, just drank the tea, and got up from my seat. When I came out of the chairman's office, comrade Jang Jae-cheol introduced some of the people who had come out there, including a university professor and a politician, and we exchanged greetings.

There, I also happened to meet Mr. Kwon No-gap, who was the secretary of Mr. Kim Dae-jung, the opposition candidate in the last presidential election.

That person took me to a coffee shop, saying let's have a coffee. He said that he had gone to Busan the other day and looked for my house but couldn't find it and just came back up, and he was very glad to see me even though it was our first meeting.

He introduced himself, saying he was currently in charge of organizing the Unification Researcher Association. Comrade Seo Beom-yong, who appeared I don't know when, came into the coffee shop and greeted me. He asked if I would be staying in Seoul for several days. I said I was planning to go down on the night train tonight.

Our group brought up the topic of the world these days. Mr. Kwon No-gap said that the Unification Association had almost formed all its provincial chapters, but the Busan Metropolitan City chapter had not yet been formed, so he asked me to either find a chapter head or, if not, to take charge of the chapter myself.

I thought about what to do about their request and said that I would take a bundle of the Unification Researcher Association's application forms and publications as they gave them.

Comrade Jang Jae-cheol is talking with a middle-aged man in a hanbok, saying there is a major announcement at 6 p.m. today but he doesn't know what kind of announcement it is. The middle-aged man in the hanbok introduced himself to me first after a while. That person's profession was a church pastor.

It's a name I've seen a lot in the newspapers. All the people who had shared the table were concerned about the country. It was nearly 6 o'clock. We carefully turned on the transistor radio. We listened intently.

The government had issued Emergency Measure No. 1. Our group was surprised by the contents of Emergency Measure No. 1. The expressions of the people around us turned serious. Sighs burst out from here and there, and desperate words came out.

I felt my heart growing cold as ice. I declined the kindness of the people there who asked me to have dinner together and started walking towards the station even though there was still a lot of time left before the train.

Bumping into people on the street, I found myself in front of Seoul Station, I don't know when I arrived. I purchased one ticket to Busan at the ticket office and entered a nearby restaurant.

 맞춰며 I was tilting soju glasses by myself, waiting for the train time. After finishing one bottle of soju, it was time for the train. Perhaps because of my emotions, I don't think I'm getting drunk. I absentmindedly found the designated seat number on the train ticket and sat down.

I kept feeling stuffy and frustrated. I called a person selling things on the train and bought a bottle of soju. Without any side dishes, I poured the soju bottle down my throat. The alcohol kept flowing down my throat.

Only then, in a hazy consciousness, did I begin to feel the potent poison of the alcohol. I had passed out drunk, leaning against the chair, unaware of the things that happened all night.

Someone shook me. When I opened my eyes, the train car was empty. I hurriedly packed my things and ran dazedly toward the station exit.

My wife, who had been worrying just in case, saw my return home and showed a very relieved expression. Day after day, the newspapers published the names of people arrested for violating the Emergency Measures. Some were faces I remembered.

Why did they have to go to prison? The reality they faced did not feel like someone else's problem. The winter that had lost its heat felt even colder and more tedious.

I felt something like a sense of shame that I was living safely. What is the greatest human desire? Each person's thoughts may be different, but if one thinks about the future for the sake of the country, I felt that the differences in each other's thinking could not be that different.

For whom was the Yushin created, and for whom was the Emergency Measures law created? In my heart, both fear and shame gradually disappeared.

It was at this time that I regretted getting married for the first time.

I think about it at a crossroads of two paths, aside from my own difficult problems. The line from a popular song, "If I follow love, my teacher cries, and if I follow my teacher, love cries," seemed as if it were a phrase created for me.

In a complicated world, thanks to the woman called my wife, I thought I had been happy for over a year. I had endured for two years, even when faced with the initial events that threatened with the Yushin.

Is it that I've developed an immunity from living in a harsh world? As the world became harsher than the Yushin I had feared, the patriotism in my young heart began to push itself forward.

In my affection for the world, my wife's face is forgotten. Could this be my destiny? Others keep quiet, but I intend to be loud.

Conscience, people rebuke those who have that conscience as foolish. And yet, to face a greater destiny, in our poor life, I did not hesitate to do an absurd thing. I started raising funds for a new activity again.

Money was not easily obtained. People tend to ignore my just words from the start, so who would lend me money, telling me to work for the country?

In the end, in my haste, I cut off the hopeful rope that was being pulled to get just one 'jeonse' room. When I told my wife to go get that money, my wife, with a frightened face, came back with all the money we had starved and worked hard to save for a year, without saying much.

The world's sentiment is different yesterday and different today, yet I had forgotten all my own problems and was only resolved that I had to go up to Seoul with only the thought that things could not be this way, without a plan, without anything.


21. Lonely Conscience

In my head now, no thoughts of myself remained.

There was only the answer that the world should not be like this, and only two decisions: to fight injustice or to choose death in order to save one's conscience.

I felt that the only thing that was the conscience of a young person was not to turn away from a difficult homeland. To do something of value, I had to go to Seoul, and I believed that there I would find something to do.

On the morning this resolve of mine became certain, my wife, while packing my clothes into a bag, repeats the same words over and over.

She looked at me with pitiful eyes filled with tears.

She pleaded that she was now with child, so I should not think only of myself but be prudent in any action while thinking of the baby to be born and herself.

I felt that such a wife before me was endlessly pathetic. My wife followed me as I left the house to where the city bus stopped.

While turning away from my wife's face, I got onto the approaching bus. The face of my wife, who was staring at the departing bus like a person in a daze, at some point is no longer visible.

The words that she was with child lingered in my head, which had lived a lonely life.

I again took an express bus leaving Busan. The bus picks up speed and runs toward Seoul. Even while looking at the scenery flashing by outside the window, I could not chase away a certain anxious thought from my heart.

For what reason did I get on this bus? What will I do right now? Are the things I intend to do as rewarding as the suffering my family will endure? Doubts keep piling up. My head was filled with thoughts and other thoughts that oppressed those thoughts.

Is everything a person's fate? If it was an unavoidable destiny, I could not even blame my own cold heart that could not look back on itself.

The youth of a human being. There is meaning in that young life. The question arose of what people of the next generation would say about the actions of those who fought against the contradictions of this land and died because they were born in this land.

I felt that to love one's country and to offer affection for that country would never be a lonely act.

My action of leaving home, abandoning myself and giving up work for myself, did not seem to be only wrong.

In my chest, neither the pitiful face of my wife nor the face of the baby to be born into the world for the first time could possibly be as great as my affection for my homeland.

With a thorny path ahead, I was feeling a deeper affection for my homeland. In my heart, I was shouting that I loved my homeland more than my wife or child.

At that moment, the express bus reached the terminal in Seoul. I thought for a good while about who I should talk to first about what was in my heart, and what came to mind was the face of my older acquaintance, Gu Jwa-seok.

In the middle of the day in Seoul, after transferring city buses twice and wandering, I was able to meet Gu Jwa-seok near the Seobu Police Station.

He looks at me with a strange gaze, perhaps wondering what to think of my action of coming all the way to his workplace.

The two of us went into a nearby cheap tavern and began to empty soju bottles even before the sun had set. As my stomach filled with alcohol, my body feels the burden. The two of us, only after the night grew dark, caught a taxi.

We got a room at an inn in Moraenae, near Gu Jwa-seok's house. I talked about my motive for coming up to Seoul at the inn.

Our conversation lasted all night. In the middle of my story, he, perhaps sobering up from the alcohol, wore a sorrowful expression. He seemed to be thinking about the law concerning the Emergency Measures.

After thinking for a good while, he opens his mouth. He said his friendship would follow me, but since the sentiment of the world is changing, how could my will possibly be achieved?

I said that I intended to follow the truth and that, with the conscience to live in a society where common sense prevails, now was the time to devote myself to the problems of my homeland, stating my resolve.

He just listened. In my heart, I thought, this person too is now thinking about the law called the Emergency Measures.

A law that could send hated people to prison for 20 years.

Is there a guarantee that my actions will not leave misunderstandings for other people? And yet, Gu Jwa-seok knew my deep affection for my country. Because he too had had a time like mine.

In 1963, as an action to oppose the Korea-Japan talks, he himself had gone to a gathering place where a crowd was assembled at a certain place in Gwangju, South Jeolla Province, and even while seeing the gazes of the people, he slit his own stomach with a knife he had prepared, and at the moment his intestines spilled out of his body and he lost consciousness, he shouted to oppose the Korea-Japan talks, a man of action with a manly spirit who cherished affection for the country's future.

I, thinking of his simple disposition, always asked back that we should create an opportunity to die for our country, and he spoke of his experience that only in the moment one resolves to die can one stick a knife in one's own stomach with one's own hand and cut it open.

His kind of story, that life is more painful than death.

I felt empathy for his heart, and I consulted with him about any of my thoughts. In doing so, we came to know that our conscience and sense of justice were in agreement, and we became even closer.

Only late in the morning did we part. He to his workplace, and I got on a bus heading toward the Jongno area. Seoul, which I had come up to after half a year, had changed a lot.

Even Mr. Wolpa Seo Min-ho, who had been so confident about his health to me, had left the world, leaving behind the dream he had.

What also made me sad were the facts that people who had been making a fuss as if they were patriots until yesterday had degenerated to gain the love under power. Seeing all this reality, I could not just blame the world.

My intention to do something for my homeland in the midst of harsh public sentiment was more foolish than trying to catch fish on a mountain. For me, it was just impossible to buy the will of the people of Seoul in a single moment.

With a heart that couldn't be helped, I went to see Chairman Kim Chul of the Unification Socialist Party.

Chairman Kim Chul was sitting on a creaky old chair in the Unification Socialist Party office, and upon seeing my face, he stood up from his seat and, smiling as always, took my hand. At that moment, I felt a sense of purpose and told him the reason I had come up to Seoul.

While listening to my story, Chairman Kim Chul told me to think carefully, yet the expression on his face immediately wavers.

Feeling as if I had met someone to talk to after a long time, I resolutely expressed my will.

"If the situation of the homeland like today was the will of heaven, then the actions I want to take are also the will of heaven. The truth is simple."

To make my will understood, I spoke to another person for the first time about my life of overcoming past despair. The party members there, who had gathered one by one in front of my face, also listened to my story.

I said that a society where common sense is extinguished is the most painful despair in my entire life.

Chairman Kim Chul offered me words of comfort. To have strength, and so, with my strength, he suggested I first try to rebuild the Minsacheong (Civilian Socialist Youth League) that belonged to that place. Immediately, the young executive party officials of the Unification Socialist Party who were next to me responded favorably.

With newfound courage, I wandered the streets of Seoul and also made contact with comrades from the days of the Daejung Party, and I attempted to make contact with young party members within the Democratic Unification Party with whom I personally connected.

I also met with pastors, former professors, and former journalists who called themselves dissident figures. I also wandered around searching for the whereabouts of comrades-in-arms I had come to know during my military service who resided in Seoul.

I held on to every person I met and appealed to them to help my actions, saying that I would not bring disgrace upon the country and that it was not defiance against the regime but for its self-reflection.

As a result of doing so, rather than a force of sympathizers, about 40 people who understood me emerged in Seoul. Now, if I just chose an appropriate day, formed the rebuilding of the Minsacheong, and went through the legal procedure of confirming the results, it seemed that any kind of talk would be possible.

Because of the earnest desire to illuminate the darkening society with youthful passion, I had no time to rest in order to complete my work quickly.

At a gathering near the Yeomcheon Bridge near Seoul Station, where I had an old comrade-in-arms nicknamed "Lightning" gather four tough-looking thugs, I tried to persuade them, asking if they wouldn't like to do something worthwhile now that they were out in the world, and while drinking free alcohol they said they would follow whatever I did, but at the part where I said I would rebuild the Minsacheong, the faces of those next to me instantly changed into frightened ones feeling terror.

For a moment, in front of them, I could not find the words in my passionate speech to reassure them about the reality.

It seems the law concerning the Emergency Measures, which had dragged so many conscientious people to prison, had grated on the nerves of not just me but anyone who had ever worried about the future of the homeland.

No challenge or just criticism could escape this law. I am trying to burn my passion and affection to challenge this reality. At such times, around me, there were people who encouraged me to have courage, and conversely, there were also those who were displeased.

In the midst of that, things happen. A few of the people who had met me without missing a day, for some reason, their faces were not seen at all that day.

I had a suspicion that these people were now avoiding me, but I thought it couldn't be. Because in my heart, their faces are the faces of people I want to believe in, even in this scary world.

As the afternoon came, I wandered the streets from my side, looking for them. Then, at a chance place, I met Professor Kim (former head of student affairs at Hongik University) who had attended my wedding.

Professor Kim, saying he had heard the news about me, holds my hand and welcomes me very much. And then, looking at the sky where there was still time left before the sun set, he pulled me into a small shop on the roadside.

Around the two of us, people with familiar faces soon gather and form a group. Glasses filled with soju were passed to me from the hands of this person and that person. The glasses of alcohol I received from several people made me, on an empty stomach, drunk. I gradually begin to lose my reason because of the alcohol.

The day was already dark, and everyone, feeling the effects of the alcohol, got up from their seats. I parted with the people and wandered the streets alone.

I felt an endless loneliness. Passersby bump into my staggering shoulders. The world was spinning round and round.

Truly, my heart wanted to go mad at that time. My heart that thinks of the homeland felt a pain as if I would die from frustration.

As I wandered without purpose, it became 12 o'clock. Because of the curfew, I returned to my lodging, the inn. Even my confused mind could be forgotten for a brief moment in deep sleep.

I was awakened by the sound of someone knocking fiercely on the door. I unlatched the doorknob, wondering who it could be. The door was flung open and there were men who rushed in.

No matter how much I shook my head, they were all faces I didn't know. Those people glare at me ferociously. And now those men, with skilled actions, hurried on to the next task.

They search every nook and cranny of the room. When I, who had been in a daze, asked who they were, they don't even answer.

Just as I was about to protest what they were doing, one of them requested that I accompany them. At that moment, sensing something, I said nothing. Thinking they were not people who would treat me with respect if I refused, I followed them.

The car we were in sped along, shining its lights on the dark asphalt road of the dawn. I couldn't even guess where we were going. But I didn't want to ask either.

When the car stopped, it was inside a building I had never been to before. I soon passed through a wide office and found myself confined in a small room that seemed to be an interrogation room. I sit alone in front of a large table and think. I could not understand the reason why I had to come into this place.

Only around 9 o'clock did the man who was called the chief among the group come into the interrogation room.

My empty stomach, from having skipped meals all day yesterday and only drinking alcohol, was sore and painful.

I, holding back the laughter that was about to come out at how ridiculous my situation was, brought up the subject first.

When I spat out the words, "If you're going to find something out, isn't it polite to at least feed a person breakfast after bringing them here?" the man called the chief, without a word of reply, immediately stood up and went outside.

Not long after, two bowls of good-quality gomtang (beef bone soup) were brought in. And the man called the chief who had gone out a moment ago comes back in.

The first thing he says to me is to have my meal. The two men, sitting facing each other, began to eat the gomtang diligently. After emptying one bowl, I felt that it had more meat than a restaurant in the city. I began to feel quite alive, as if I would live.

Now I was curious and frustrated about the things that would happen next. An ominous feeling keeps arising. My gaze began to search every nook and cranny of the small room.

What time could it be? After thinking about the time, I spoke up, saying I wanted to go urinate outside. After a little while, a man with broad shoulders sticks to my side and accompanies me. In the empty hallway, he asked a question of interest.

"Sir, you were involved in the movement, weren't you?"

I put on a serious face.

He, saying he knew everything, told me the story of Lightning, who was a tough guy in front of Seoul Station. On the way back, I saw the faces I had been searching for so much in the Jongno area yesterday in this office.

They looked at me with bloodshot eyes and exhausted expressions. As I entered the interrogation room, I sent them a nod of greeting.

I sat alone all midday without being asked any questions. It became noon.

Again, a bowl of appetizing gomtang was brought in. After putting the meat broth and food into my stomach on time after a long while, I felt even more lively.

All sorts of thoughts came to my mind. What are these people going to do with me? The ominous thought does not cease to disappear from my head.

The face of my wife and the face of my unborn child came to mind. Will it be a boy or a girl? For what reason was I dragged to a place like this? And yet, the other side still neither asks nor answers.

I wondered if they were going to create some kind of conspiracy after making me anxious. Thinking of a bird trapped in a cage, I felt the recklessness of resisting in this place. It was in the afternoon that I felt a shiver in my head and had a fearful thought.

My still innocent heart was on the side of believing the world and believing people. Even while believing that I would be safe, seeing the attitude of the people here, it was not clear.

Whether I was lucky or not, only late at night was I able to leave that unpleasant place, receiving greetings even from people different from those who had taken me.

The next day, half by others' will and half by my own, I packed my bag and went to the Dongdaemun Express Bus Terminal. I did not tell anyone that I was leaving Seoul.

Comrade Lee Dong-yeol, who came to see me early in the morning because he was worried about my recent situation, and comrade Kim Sang-seok, who had served as a steering committee member of the National Congress for the Restoration of Democracy, carried my bag to the terminal, and we mutually hid our deep regret.

The three of us, in front of a handcart near the terminal, offered each other glasses of farewell with a bottle of soju and two boiled eggs. The warmth of the alcohol rose up, making me flush.

The bus for Busan started its engine to depart. I got on the bus, and the two men went to where they had to go. In the moving bus, I began to pray in my heart.

O God, take away the curse from this land and bestow your blessing.

Truly, my heart wished for my fellow countrymen to obtain even a small happiness through God's will. The express bus arrived at the Busan terminal late in the afternoon.

After having no contact at all during that time, I suddenly enter the house, and my wife, who was tutoring a room full of children, gets tears in her eyes and, telling the children to come tomorrow, sends them home earlier than the scheduled time, and then she asks if I had any hardships, stealing glances at my face.

My feeling as I watched the back of my wife leaving for the kitchen area, after she spread out a mattress in the room and recommended that I lie down for a bit to relieve my fatigue, was that my heart ached with the thought that I had gotten married for no good reason and was just making an innocent woman suffer.

Now I thought that I should pay some attention to the life of us two poor people.

The sentiment of the world was changing frighteningly day by day. I started to wander the streets, wondering if there was any suitable business for me.

I would walk from Yeongdo to Seomyeon and then walk back.

Despite trying so hard, no suitable job came into my sight. I had to start something, but in my head, I couldn't even dare to think what to do.

Half a year passed without any news from Seoul. We welcomed the Lunar New Year of 1975.

My wife, now in her final month of pregnancy, seemed to be worried about preparing for childbirth. My mother-in-law frequently visited the house we were renting.

Then one day, my wife had an easy delivery. The baby was a boy. For some reason, the heart that should have been happy was not happy. To me, who had no ability to make a living, it was just one more mouth to feed.

The small room now became even more uncomfortable as we had to make a sleeping space for the baby as well.

My wife and I, to endure as a family of three in the small room, lay down in opposite directions when we went to bed.

My toes always played near my wife's head, and my wife's toes played under my nose, and my wife says my feet smell because I don't wash them. The two of us were mindful for each other's sake.

Winter again turned into spring. The baby turned 100 days old. He began to smile quite a bit.

My wife's expression showed that it was getting harder and harder to manage the household of three people with no income. Even though I couldn't say it, as a man, I felt as if my pride was in shambles. I make a resolution.

Fine. Let me find myself. From that day on, I hastened my efforts to prove that poverty was not my friend. I met people relentlessly. Still, no clear solution had yet been found.

One day, I was supposed to meet a friend at Namdo Dabang in front of City Hall. Having not set a time, I waited for him vaguely for several hours.

Two customers come and sit at the table next to mine. The two people talk in a voice loud enough to be heard at the next table. The talk seemed to be about business.

Being bored, my interest naturally turned to that conversation. The people at the next table continued their conversation, even emitting heat. In the middle of their conversation, I thought about whether I could also start such a business.

The two people, in the height of their excitement, chatted away and then went outside. In my head, the story I had just overheard lingered. At that moment, the friend I was waiting for came into the coffee shop. He sits down in the seat across from me, saying he's been waiting a long time.

When I jokingly asked that friend about the story I had just heard that kept lingering in my head and wouldn't be erased, he said that someone he knew a little was doing that kind of business and asked if there would be a suitable place in Busan city.

From the next day, thinking of the story I heard and my friend's advice as my capital, I wandered the seaside alone. Truly, as my friend said, there was no place. But I did not give up.

It was one such day. At the border between Nambumin-dong and Amnam-dong, I discovered a place where a boat could be docked. The place I saw was a remote location in the situation at that time.

That day, I returned home and consulted with my wife. I also went to see my older sister and brother and talked to them, thinking it might be of help, but this was a mistake.

Just because I said a few words asking if I could get some money, my older brother came to see me the next morning and, so that the people around could hear, he shouted that I was a bandit-like bastard, a bastard who doesn't know his place, and broke my spirit.

He says, "There are many people in the world who can fly and crawl, do you think a thing like you can fly without wings?" and gets angry for no reason, even spitting on my face in front of my wife before leaving.

My older brother came again the next morning and cursed at me. However, I am not the kind of person to give up what I have to do just because the situation is difficult.

I ran around to learn the sea routes and also went to see people related to such work. And then I diligently mulled it over by myself.

The thought of what to do if I couldn't get the money also came up. My wife, for her part, seemed to be worried. They say that when you're drowning, you want to cling to even a single straw of hay, and since I, who had only been doing absurd things, got my act together and said I would try to do business, it seemed her expectations were still high.

The two of us, while worrying about money, could not show it. We tried not to disappoint each other.

22. To Get Bread

My wife, I don't know where she got it, but she came back having secured the 400,000 won I needed.

As I had been thinking, I started work, rushing into action without hesitation.

The 400,000 won was absolutely insufficient for the sand price, the barge fee, the outer ship fee, and the land unloading fee, but my situation was such that I had to give it a try. In the end, I borrowed a boat and went up the Seomjin River.

News that I, a gangster who was quite well-known in Busan city, was starting a sand business quickly spread on the Seomjin River.

While the crane loaded sand from under the water onto the barge all night long, I didn't sleep a wink and loitered by the side of the boat to load even a little more sand onto it.

The next day, the barge owner, waking from his sleep, saw the sand loaded on the boat and flew into a rage, saying the boat was too heavily loaded to sail. I talked him down and brought the boat out.

The outer ship captain was also quite cooperative, perhaps because of my face. Believing only the outer ship captain's word that the boat could arrive in Busan by midday tomorrow, I went up to the land side and came down to Busan first by car.

I rushed around to many places to find a crane for unloading sand so that I could immediately unload the sand when the boat arrived.

There were many awkward things as it was my first time doing this, but in the end, everything was taken care of.

When the news got out that I was doing business, 20 comrades who cared for me and people around me came out to watch and set up a drinking party on the seashore.

At the scheduled time, the boat was seen on the sea. Both the shipowner waiting on the seashore and I were relieved. The barge reached the harbor, and the prepared crane began to lift the sand onto the land.

At that moment, a man who called himself the land manager came to find me, panting, and began to argue. He was furious, asking whose permission I had to unload sand here. That person began to obstruct the work.

The 20 or so friends and comrades who had gathered around, without knowing the reason, rebuked the manager.

The manager, who was jumping up and down, seemed to be calling the landholding company. Even though a dispute had arisen, with the help of the people around and my own nerve, I was able to completely finish the work the next morning.

Seeing the pile of sand, people who lived in that neighborhood came to ask for work. I decided to hire three people who said they lived nearby.

I couldn't entrust the site supervision to just anyone, so I pleaded with my father-in-law from my wife's family to come out and watch over the goods, and I received his help.

For the first day, no one came to buy the goods. I became frustrated and ran around to construction sites to get orders.

For a few days, nothing sold except for the orders I had taken myself. As time went by, one or two regular customers began to be secured.

For the business, I woke up from bed at 3:30 in the morning.

After roughly washing my face and eating about a bowl of rice in soup, it would be 4 o'clock. On any given day, at that time, I would rush out of the house and run to the site.

In the empty plain where no one was visible because of the early hour, I gathered scattered pieces of wood to light a fire and waited for the workers and waited for the customers. The effect appeared.

The workers, seeing the owner hurrying, also came out earlier than the workers at other places. Early in the morning, when trucks came to get goods, they came to us and bought them because there was no one at the other places.

As the business got on track, I faithfully supervised at the site until the sun set and the work was finished.

When it was lunchtime, I boiled a single packet of ramen in front of the workers and made do with that for lunch, and for dinner, I always came home to eat. The business I started unfolded more smoothly than I had thought. The profit from selling the first boatload was more satisfying than I had thought.

From then on, I no longer needed to go to the Seomjin River myself to buy sand. This was because barge owners would load sand and come to me asking me to take it.

Then it was one day. Employees of the landholding company where the sand was stored appeared. I had expected it, but they said troublesome things.

I pleaded until my hands and feet were worn out, begged them to help me, saying I would pay the rent as I could afford, and at times, I also acted boldly. Then, as time passed, the disputes with the landowners also lessened.

But then another problem arose. It was because the nearby Maritime High School had lent out the land they had reclaimed for a playground to four other people to do sand business for expensive rent. From then on, the business competition was fierce. The weak would fail and the strong could endure.

I had to compete in this environment. At times like these, my face had many advantages. Perhaps because of my reputation, regular customers also increased.

As experience accumulated, confidence overflowed in my heart. My grit and eloquence were not lacking in carrying out the role of one diligent worker and the president.

The workers were amazed. The workers, who used to pack and eat their lunch boxes, seemed dumbfounded to see me boiling and eating ramen.

Also, as the work gradually increased, some of the initial clumsy workers from the neighborhood couldn't stand the hard work more than the income and quit. The newly replaced workers were all skilled at shoveling.

Because I had experienced heavy labor from a young age, my talent for handling people on a labor site could be called heaven-sent.

Even the older people who came out to work feigned death in front of me. I always handled work and personal matters separately.

Finally, results began to appear before me. In just a few months, we had paid off the debt we were carrying, and with my own capital, the pile of sand grew larger and larger.

For the first time, my wife's family began to trust me. They had seen me as just a gangster, but their attitude changed after hearing my father-in-law's story.

The business I started without experience began to find its footing in less than 6 months. Seeing my actions, I began to worry again from that point.

A dilemma arose at the crossroads of whether to earn money or to save the country. The urgent and hungry days kept coming into view. And on the other hand, a society that does not take responsibility even when promises are broken added to the distress in my eyes.

I, who grew up in sorrow and darkness, realized a strong conscience that I must save the darkening society even if it meant making my family cry.

At last, I came to know what my mission was. The flame that had been dormant in my chest rose again and began to burn my heart. I resolved that I must first unite sincere people and save the ailing society.

Before my eyes, problems were arising between people due to the world's distrust. It was heartbreaking to see that consciences beaten by injustice were not protected by the law.

The most important thing was the fact that social justice was not visible and that power was replacing justice. An even more frightening fact was what I learned after knowing the legal interpretation of the principle that investigative agencies should not intervene in civil cases.

For conscientious people, it was as useless as poison, and for evil people with a disposition for swindling, it was as effective as a tonic, so shameless evil people would take whatever they could, commit fraud, and swindle, calling it a civil matter.

The law says one should just claim fairness. The good person is disgraced. The one who was robbed resents the one who robbed.

People gradually lost their affection for society, and a habit of making hasty judgments arose, saying "they're all the same," and not trying to see the world correctly. Seeing this, my heart is torn by a mixture of anger and sadness.

I felt the urgent sense that saving the disappearing justice and the collapsing national spirit was more urgent than my family life.

After worrying about this for several days, I decided to take half of my business capital and use it for this purpose. The very first thing I started was to find an office.

After wandering around real estate agencies here and there, I signed a contract for a 5-pyeong office near the Bupyeong-dong intersection for a deposit of 100,000 won and a monthly rent of 20,000 won.

I borrowed someone else's phone and got old furniture from people I knew and brought it in. Now, another decision was needed.

I had to pour passion into whatever I did. It was a question of whether to go out to the business site or to wander the streets in search of comrades.

I made a decision without thinking further. I wandered the streets in search of people. I conveyed my intentions to everyone I met and tried to persuade them.

I also thought deeply about what method I should use to offer my affection to the people of this land. Then one day, I decided to form the Youth Council for the Expulsion of Social Absurdities.


23. Action to Find Justice

To create a social organization to fight injustice, I first had to create a statement of purpose and articles of association. Also, thinking that this time I must start in Busan and push the influence northward, I also looked for things that would suit that condition.

I made membership application forms and handed them out to people I met around me, persuading them to save society. In less than a week of such action, about 40 people became founding members and responded favorably.

I entrusted the drafted statement of purpose and articles of association to a printing shop and had them print several thousand copies. At last, to launch the council, I convened the people who had submitted membership applications.

In the cramped office, with 35 founding members present, we formed the executive board of the Youth Council for the Expulsion of Social Absurdities, a task we had been promoting until now in order to take action.

On the motion of comrade Jeong Gap-deok, the chairman of the Buddhist Orators' Council who had attended as a founding member, I was elected as the chairman of that council by unanimous vote that evening. On my motion, the heads of each department were approved from among the members.

The attendees discussed duties and actions, and a firm resolve began to form in the hearts of the gathered people. They knew it was natural for young people to take the lead for the social problems of their homeland, and they knew it was a rewarding thing to spread justice.

However, from the next day, I came to realize how arduous it was for young people to act on their conscience for their homeland.

People working for the government agencies came to see me with flustered faces. I gave those people a copy of the statement of purpose and articles of association. I also explained the work I intended to do. Those people, to my face, told me to do a good job, but I had a strange premonition.

In the end, I could feel that my hopes, without even being able to start, were met with the cold shoulder and despair of those around me. As I hurried to register the social organization, strange things that I hadn't even thought of began to happen around me.

I pressed the secretary-general, comrade Jeong Gap-deok, who was knowledgeable about administration, to submit the registration documents to the relevant ministry and establish a legal status, but a few days later, for some reason, the only news that came back was that the registration process might be impossible.

I finally had a premonition that no young conscience could devote itself to the homeland and society. From then on, another ordeal began for me. As if to add insult to injury, misfortunes occurred consecutively.

It was a notification from the boat owners that they could not deliver sand to our yard. A boat that was supposed to come to our yard under contract had to turn back due to external pressure while waiting at the yard.

Not knowing the reason, I ran around here and there, burning with anxiety. I simply could not understand the things happening around me.

Also, a dangerous thing happened to one of our comrades. As unfortunate events began to appear continuously, people got scared one by one and avoided meeting me.

Only then did I begin to feel lonely. Before a few days had passed, I was eventually left alone to guard the office. I felt a strange feeling as I looked over the thousands of printed copies of the statement of purpose and articles of association.

I shook my head, unable to understand the world at all. It was a case of being beaten after setting out to catch a thief.

What went wrong? I scanned the contents of the statement of purpose.

<Today, we are at a historical turning point, struggling to find future glory and hope in a turbulent vortex.

Crying out for revolution, economy, reform, the Yushin system, and so on, can all be said to be a struggle to wisely achieve the glory and development of the homeland and the nation in a turbulent vortex.

It is a certain and undeniable fact that our society is changing day by day. Some call this a great achievement, while others assert it is a failure.

This is due to the difference in views depending on the worldview of those who criticize and their interpretation of democracy, so we have no business getting involved here.

However, there is an important and urgent matter here that we must not overlook and must resolve quickly.

That is none other than the elimination of social absurdities.

In this urgent time when a storm may rage and a volcano may erupt, shaking the land at any time, anywhere in today's turbulent world, the reality of today, where social absurdities have infiltrated every corner of society and are deeply rooted, casts an ominous shadow on the future of our nation.

Thinking back, this enormous fact that such social absurdities are rooted deep in the innermost parts of our society is dealing a fatal, decisive blow to the future of our nation.

This is because social absurdities are incompatible with the glory and development of the nation. Therefore, anyone who is a member of a nation that takes pride and self-esteem in the glorious history of their homeland yearns to cleanly sweep the social absurdities from our society.

However, social absurdities are too deeply rooted. Therefore, everyone knows the fact that eliminating this cannot be done overnight, nor can it be easily resolved even by mobilizing the administrative power of the state.

The elimination of social absurdities cannot be achieved by the power of the state, nor can it be done by just boasting with words. The only way is to launch a national moral reform movement from a new dimension and with a new resolve.

However, such a grand national movement is too enormous a task and not an easy problem.

Therefore, we deeply examine our own intentions, set a scope appropriate to our capabilities, and with the passion and clean heart of young people, we intend to gather the will of like-minded youths to form the Youth Council for the Expulsion of Social Absurdities.

Year 1975, Month, Day

Contents of the Articles of Association

Chapter 1 General Provisions

Article 1 (Name) This council shall be called the Youth Council for the Expulsion of Social Absurdities.

Article 2 (Purpose) The purpose of this council is to carry out a bold social movement to eliminate corruption and social absurdities in various fields.

Article 3 (Projects) This council shall carry out the following projects to achieve its purpose.

1. Campaign movement for the construction of a fair and just society.
2. Movement to promote a society where sincere people can live well.
3. Movement to warn, expose, and report on social absurdities.

Article 4 (Location) The central headquarters of this council shall be in Busan, and branches shall be established in Seoul Metropolitan City and in each provincial capital city as needed.>

The letters blur in my eyes as I read down the articles of association. Tears had welled up.

Facing my own cruel fate, I wonder if I truly cannot work anywhere for a valuable happiness.

Even while watching the conduct of those around me who try to live by being observant, I had no way of knowing what kind of man of destiny I was to be choosing and rushing into only losing propositions.

At night, people see the same darkness, but what kind of damn fate is it that I alone act with such burning anxiety?

Is it a man's conscience to cause worry to his wife and see the gaunt face of his child? Anger welled up at the world, but lamentation for myself also arose.
